RATING: 8
If it's possible, this is both derivative and innovative at the same time. The worker placement is nothing new at all (and it's far from one of the best worker placement systems) but the theme is absoultely fabulous and really immerses you in the building of a dungeon - an Ameritrash game with euro mechanics if ever there was one.

RATING: 8
Huh, I never commented on this one? This is a very solid game. If you like Vlada as a designer, you need to pick this one up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Like so many of Vlaada's designs, initial head-scratching gives way to satisfyingly intuitive gameplay once digested. It took the majority of the first game for it to really click for my (fairly experienced) group though. 

The 'bookkeeping' can seem fiddly at first but it soon becomes pretty fluid and automatic. 

I love the feel of this game just as much as Dungeon Petz. The simultaneous action selection is just as good here, but tighter as you have to commit to the specific spots in advance. Decent mix of tactical play and long-term strategy. You definitely have to pay attention to other people's boards and available actions. I think repeated plays will be rewarded with knowledge of the room tiles, traps etc. and generally just getting better at managing both planning and combat; I think we will see people gunning for those 5 point paladins in future! 

On the downside, it really shines at 4p which means I don't get it to the table as much as I'd like. And it's a bit of a pain to teach as you do need to cover pretty much everything in order for any of the decisions to make sense. Still, it's a keeper. A Dungeon Keeper. har har
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
I did not enjoy my first play of Dungeon Lords. There's a bit of a gimmick with worker placement - but it doesn't work terribly well for me. You simultaneously select and the positions of most value are not all the same - e.g. first placement is not always best. It doesn't lead to a tough decision because it's controlled (for the most part) by chaos. The best portion of play was combat - which seems to be governed mainly by monster selection. And as has been said before, you pretty much just play the game twice in a row. Finally, there's no way this game plays in 90 minutes. Ours ran more like 4 hours. Over all, a fair game, but not great.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.7
Dungeon Keeper, el juego PC ahora en tu mesa! XD Interesante mecanica de selección de acciones, de gestión, y segunda parte del juego de "lucha/puzzle" entre los aventureros y tus trampas y monstruos. Esta entretenido,  y divertido si le metes el tema que llevan las instrucciones. Tienes pocos turnos para gestionarte, y como crítica, tomas pocas decisiones para lo que dura el juego. Por ello tienes que planificar bien tus recursos y la lucha al final de cada uno los 2 años que dura el juego. Optimizado para 4, se puede jugar a 3 o 2 con las reglas oficiales, colocando únicamente en cada turno los esbirros de los otros jugadores virtuales, cosa que a 2 jugadores, cada uno maneja a otro más, sólo las ordenes, y resulta interesante. A 3 no lo he probado.

RATING: 9.5
Another Vlaada gem. It's a pure euro that also has a great theme, superb components, very nice mechanics and a thoroughly enjoyable experience for me. It's pretty complex and many things can be taken into consideration each turn, but because of the high amount of decisions, the final fight at the end of the round is pretty interesting, as many things don't work the way we'd like them to :) Still, although the learning curve it not that lean (especially for the combat rounds) once learned things work pretty fast. It's a medium-to-complex euro game that brings an original theme and some very cool ideas. Highly recommended.

RATING: 6
Not my favorite Vlaada Chvátil game. You play a aspiring dungeon lord who is part of this sort of fantasy corporation. You get promoted based on how well you do at constructing a dungeon that thwarts adventurers. While the premise is neat the execution isn't fantastic. The game has a lot of upkeep regarding resolutions and often feels more like a lot of set up and then some combat happens and then a new round begins. There also isn't a big variety of monsters or heroes so the game can feel played out and repetitious after a while. That being said it is a beautiful game and it does have some interesting stuff going on. It is just out competed by new additions to my game collection so I don't bring it to the table anymore.

RATING: 8
So.  Awesome.  Build up your dungeon for when the adventurers arrive.  Watch your eviltude on the evilometer lest the Paladin seek you out.  This one just oozes theme.

Lots of fun.  I like the action selection mechanic.  Limited actions leading up to "scoring rounds" (combat rounds) lend tension to the design.  The bits and artwork are magnificent!  Game length is just right to a little long.  I like how there's a concentrated delay (choosing actions, everyone at once) followed by quick resolution.  Game flows fairly well.

Loses points for fiddliness and lack of a catch up mechanism.  Very difficult to come back and not too hard to fall behind.  Also, teaching this is a pain.  There's a lot to cover and it tends to take awhile.

Still, I love this game!  Strongly recommended that you give it a try and see if it's for you.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I played this game just twice, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Very cool idea and interesting mechanics. This is the first worker placement game that I tried and it immidiately made me want to find a game of this category good for my taste, because, even if I loved this mechanic right away, I didn't love Dungeon Lords as much. I see in this game a lot of good elements: I like worker placement, I like the deduction element and I like the fact that I can "build" something.

Yet the mix doesn't work so well for me. The building part is actually very small and a little too random (I'm talking about the Rooms). The number of decision is also very low, which for someone can be an interesting challenging element, but I don't like it. The scoring also is wierd: I like the idea of the titles, but I don't understand what's the point of counting the number of caught adventurers, which is the same for everyone (if you don't count the big guy). In the end the graphic looks also a little too messy and wierd and makes me dislike the visual impact of the game.

The entire game gives me a strange feeling of good mechanics, that are not so well put together. It gives me some fun and I might play it every now and then, but probably it doesn't match my taste enough to make me enjoy it more.

RATING: 7
Preliminary rating. Vlaada Chvátil is unquestionably brilliant, and Dungeon Lords hosts stunning artwork, charming and high quality components, and hilarious theming. But is it fun? Procedural plus chaos doesn't seem to be my thing: the chaos of worker placement uncertainty would be OK in a less lengthy, less involved game, but here feels unsatisfying, if not outright irritating because of the enormous and lingering impact. Highly intricate with lots of small fiddly rules that I suspect would become second nature after more plays, but I'm not sure more plays are in the offing for me. Like Space Alert, this is another Vlaada game I'm probably destined to admire more than enjoy...

Extra point for the Evilometer. Love that!

RATING: 9
Bought this on artwork, theme and BGG rating.  Didn't play as I expected but my respect and enjoyment is increasing with each play.

Despite the different themes, I'd describe it as very similar to Agricola.  It's all about worker placement.  Dungeon building and fighting off heroes is a secondary concern.

Only played 4 times, so this could easily change to an 8 or 10 in future.  Too bad it doesn't go to 5 players, I'll be playing the 3 player game mostly.

Loving it as a 3-4 player game but find the 2 player too brutal.  Don't think I'll be playing "divorce mode" very often.

RATING: 7.5
Played it years ago, and recall having the initial reaction that it was a good game, but the replay ability would be poor since you see all the monsters etc every game.  I've read that was an intentional design decision for a tighter balance, but still one I think makes the game less fun than it could be.

I recently saw the expansion on a substantial discount and decided to get it and the base game for myself, so I'm hoping the expansion will add some replay ability to it, but even so, its a good game to play a couple times a year.

Probably never gonna happen, but would love to see the theme redone to just focus on the dungeon building/management aspect of the game since that's where the draw is for me.

RATING: 4
A lot of mechanics and rules melded into this game.  Basically you simultaneously draft various actions in order to build up a dungeon which will (hopefully) be capable of holding off the various adventurers seeking plunder and glory from you.  A game for gamers in that there is a bit of rules teaching that needs to be done beforehand, taking some time to get to actually playing.  If anything the game is very strategic, offering a lot of different options to players.  However while there is a lot to do in this game, it ultimately devolves into a long and drawn-out endeavor.  I can appreciate the mechanics, but players are asked to think too much and are rewarded with too little.  I don't completely hate the game however, it's just that there are a lot of other games out there which do similar things to this and are a lot more enjoyable and play faster.

As an asides note I just wanted to give thanks to Z-Man games for sending me in some of the components missing in my game!

RATING: 4.5
Jugada otra partida a 2 jugadores que no está registrada.

Primero las cosas buenas.

- Grandísimo arte gráfico con muchos guiños y gran sentido del humor.

- Los componentes son muy bonitos y con muchos detalles, aunque eso si, solo de calidad media (tableros y cartas principalmente).

Ahora el resto

- La idea de juego es brillante, pero su puesta en práctica me ha defraudado bastante. Las reglas son demasiado farragosas para lo que luego es el juego en sí. Nada intuitivas y diría que hasta rebuscadas y difíciles de recordar (demasiadas excepciones para que el juego resulte fluído). Explicarlas a otro puede ser un suplicio para ambos.

- El juego es caótico, con apuestas ocultas que no sabes como van a resultar y por tanto resulta imposible hacer una planificación de una estrategia a corto plazo. A largo plazo ni hablamos.

- Hay 0 interacción entre jugadores. Cada uno a su mazmorra y poco más. 

- A dos jugadores es un fiasco. El parche del jugador dummy no funciona y hace el juego menos fluído todavía. Sospecho que solo fue pensado para 4 jugadores y para menos se ideó un apaño.

- El desarrollo del juego resulta bastante soso, con pocas acciones interesantes que explorar.


En definitiva, una gran idea, un muy buen grafismo pero un juego que se queda entre dos aguas.

Ignoro si con las múltiples expansiones mejorará algo. Sospecho que lo único que se conseguirá con ellas es embrollarlo más aún.

RATING: 8.4
Not quite as clever as its sibling Dungeon Petz, but also a bit heavier. You've got your Building phases and Combat phases, the former sort of a worker placement game where you're making adjustments to your dungeon, and the latter you actually fight the adventurers (and hopefully not Paladins) you have attracted. Sort of like Boss Monster except far, far more elaborate and better. The rulebook is amazing and funny, giving a clear description of every mechanic here. Loads of strategy, this game can get tough when the pressure is on and the adventurers arrive to conquer all that you've created. Good, advanced design.

RATING: 2.7
Rating based on playing twice. The game doesn't really convince (kinda fiddly; not enough meat). Overall board setup and housekeeping phases take too long. The worker-placement aspects work great (love double-guessing what everyone else will play), but with only two resolution phases in a game which takes as long as this (so playing multiple times after each other isn't really appealing for most players), you're hardly ever seeing the ultimate effect of your choices, and it's going to take forever to learn to play effectively. 
The game also constantly gives you the feeling you're doing really badly and not making progress, and mistakes are extremely costly. It's weird to have so little fun playing a game which still will have quite a few moments of everyone laughing out loudly. Could be a really good game for a group of diehards who'll really dig in, but definitely not good for something approaching casual play.

Rating might still go up if we play this more often, but the impression so far isn't really going to invite playing more often... :/

After not having felt any desire to give this another try for ~2.5 years, I traded it away for Tikal in the Essen 2012 math trade.

RATING: 7.5
Enjoyed much more than I expected.  My opponents knew the pc game Dungeon Keeper, and attested to the similarities.  But I think as a stand alone design it has much to be admired.  The card drafting technique is simple and inspired, as is the 'worker' placement.  The non random combat build up and mechanism is the cleverest I can think of.  It is clear that a huge amount of development work has gone into the design.  The rules are well drafted and written with humour.  The presentation and graphics are first rate.  My second favourite design of Vladimír Chvátil (after TTA), who never fails to impress.  At the moment (Dec-09) I can't think of an outfit that can beat the CGE team.  My only minor complaint is that I find it difficult to believe we will ever get the game down to its published 90 minute playing time, but even at double that the journey is fun.  My rating is not higher simply because I am not sure how often I want to play this style and size of game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Some games click and some games don't.  Sometimes a bad game clicks right away (it's just bad) and sometimes a great game takes a while before you get it.

Dungeon Lords does not click.  We found that no one played with any intention in the first game.  We just did stuff to see how it works.  

My game group happens to be impatient and it will be hard to convince them to sit down with this to figure it out.  It might be great but it will be difficult to get another chance to find out for sure.

One more thing.  I hate game boards that put all the interesting art work in the spots that will be covered by your pieces.  What a waste!  This game is a perfect example of that.

-------------------
-------------------

A few more plays in and it's starting to click (tho I may have ventured into the expanded game a little too quickly).  This has all the hallmarks of Vlaada's best work.  The puzzle solving, the humor, the madness!  Still a lot here to explore though.  In fact I think it's actually harder to grok than his epic: Mage Knight the Board Game.  

Will be digging in further to see if it holds up to more and more plays.

RATING: 8.5
Great theme, great artwork. Chvatil never disappoints. I've only played this twice but it was fantastic. A complex game that feels very deep, but then when it's over you're surprised that it didn't actually take very long at all. The simultaneous planning does wonders for ensuring that downtime isn't an issue and makes for great head games. It makes the game feel very interactive as you are very much focused on what you're opponents are doing and trying to outwit them. No multiplayer solitaire here, even though player interaction is very indirect. 

Rating might go significantly higher after a few more plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Feb-6-2010 - First Play
I was excited to play this game as I have been reading about great reception it got from the BGG community and I was not disappointed.  The theme is hilarious and unique and the game play is a lot of fun.  There is a lot of jokes to be made while playing the game.  The game should be taken lightly since there is a lot of chaos in the game.  The better you guess where people are going the better stuff you receive.  So if you guess right, great windfall.  If you don't, you are set back.  Also, there are a lot of moving parts in the game that I see is needed but certainly bogs down the game a lot.  The game's theme might be conducive as a gateway game but it has so many little rules that are needed that it is not a gateway game at all.  It is very apparent that the game was designed only as a 4 player game and the rest of number player games are "patched" up to work.  The game takes a long time to play but I was pretty absorbed so I didn't notice.  It wasn't until we were done playing that I realized that it took 4 hours to teach and play a game.  Hopefully, this will go so much faster as we now know the rules.  Overall, it is a fun game of puzzles and hilarity.  I'd definitely play again.
Initial Rating: 8

RATING: 7
Wonderful looking game.  However, Vlaada should have included 4 additional bits (tokens, figures, meeples) to indicate which room or tunnel the adventure party is currently conquering.  Using a Minion meeple is stupid, especially if you decide not to engage the party with a monster or ghost in that spot.  I bet players will probably pimp Dungeon Lords with an adventure party token in the same way that players pimp Agricola.

As for gameplay, I enjoyed the simultaneous card playing, but it does make the game pretty random.  It sucks when better monsters or rooms come out the turn in which you are prohibited from playing a moster or room card.  It also sucks when your traps would work better against the heroes that your opponents luck into.  One thing to point out is that I never play with Special events!  Way too random.  

Since there really are only a handful of Heroes, Monsters, & Rooms, I do wonder how much replay value is included in the box.  Dungeon Lords is already begging for an expansion.  

[b]Festival Season[/b]
I was worried that the unique opportunities would be super random, but players know in advance the next upcoming opportunity, so you can mostly plan things.  Overall I enjoyed the expansion.  It adds length.  It's also something that I'd only play with my regular gaming group for Dungeon Lords is already a pain to teach.

This game not only is about monsters, it is a monster itself! There are so many possibillities that it's almost impossible to grasp everything in a first play. Then you will have just scratched the surface of the wonderous world of Dungeon Lords. The intricacies of the game, the realization of how all the elements work together will slowly grow on you, after a lot of plays.

There is so much stuff you feel you have to do and so little time to do it in. Add to that the simultaneous action selection where you are constantly second guessing the actions your opponents will take to maximize your own and you have another winner by Chvatil. This guy is quickly becoming my favorite game designer. Very high recommendations!

RATING: N/A
This is a game with high production value and a worthy game to boot. I would recommend that any gamer who appreciates fine production values should give this a try, especially if you enjoy Galaxy Trucker.

That being said, I think this game "euro-izes" the Galaxy Trucker "build-then-destroy" model even more. As in Galaxy Trucker, you are building something first then you see how well it holds up when the bad things happen. In this game the bad things are a group of adventurers who come to your rob and pillage your dungeon.

What's different than Galaxy Trucker, besides the incredibly original theme of course, is that in this game you build through a kind of a card-programming (ala Roborally), worker placement, and resource management approach. That is a lot different than the real-time grab-and-go building in Galaxy Trucker. Additionally, you get to make more interesting decisions in the destroy phase when the adventurers come to loot your dungeon. Though some may disagree, I think this game improves on the already successful and likable Galaxy Trucker quite a bit.

I'll play it, but I'd rather play other worker placement games that don't have such a huge learning curve, yet seem to offer more in the way of strategic thinking.

RATING: 6.5
Bit of a strange one this. I came into the game with high hopes but came away slightly disappointed.

I think the problem is there are two competing games at the heart of this one. There is the nicely-balanced, Euro-style worker placement which is very well done. Then there is the daft, adventurer-bashing, dungeon-overlording game laced with Vlaada's famous sense of humour. 

Whilst they are both lovely games in their own right they do not sit well together. The part of you that responds to optimising your economy and maximising points does not always want the distraction of monsters and traps, whilst the part that loves the monsters and traps and wants to be the evilest evil-dude ever finds the fiddly placement stuff a little boring.

Who would have thought a subsistence farming theme could ever sit better on a game than a dungeon overlord theme?

RATING: 4
Like all the other games by this same author, Dungeon Lords is well overrated: in its core it’s nothing more than a mediocre eurogame with an appealing theme stuck on it, which is pretty deceptive, since while playing you never feel it’s the theme that is dictating the rules but quite the opposite, that everything had to be bent to dry and unthematic arithmetics.

In this game you’re never allowed to do what you’d like to do but you only have to be content with the half-satisfying actions you managed to take in a given turn: on top of that, it prevents any form of fight or interaction among players, save for the usual indirect forms that are so commonly found among the most boring eurogames, such as obstructing an opponent or forestalling him on the last slot available, which is the same as throwing a stone and hiding the hand that threw it. (September 2011)

[b]Update[/b] - Almost two years later I still find Dungeon Lords an annoying game: thus in July 2013 I lowered my rating from 5 to 4. Anyhow, it’s appalling to see that it’s still way better than its sequel, Dungeon Pets, which succeeds in being even dryer and more irritating than Dungeon Lords itself.

RATING: 5
How disappointing.  The idea for this game is astoundingly cool, but Vlaada pulls out another overcomplicated mess that barely qualifies as a game.

First off, the worker placement system here is awful.  Blindly assigning workers, but the chances everyone will choose the same space (the only way to be denied) is pretty slim.  Instead you might get an inferior or different version of the action.  Unfortunately, there is very little way to game this system at all.  Also, you can't choose the same actions two rounds in a row which has no thematic reason and appears to exist mostly to make the game harder.

Sadly, that is about all the player interaction involved.  The rest of the game has you defending your dungeon.  A lot of rules are involved here, but they don't make it any more fun.  Yes, there are some decisions to be made here, but they are fairly straightforward and not as puzzlelike as the rules made them out to be.

It cannot be overstated how overcomplicated this is with lots of exceptions.  You get your first order card back, just remember to pay 1 gold if it's one particular card.  Only 1 trap and 1 monster in a tunnel, 2 monsters and a trap in a room, but remember to pay a gold for that trap.  Trolls are like imps except when they are not.  Yeah it's not hard to internalize after a game, but I am not sure it adds anything and the combat section is really a bear to learn for not much payoff.

To sum up my complaints, this is almost a non-game with a weak worker placement system and very little further interaction with other players and very few interesting decisions.  It is also quite chaotic for such a long game with a the results of worker placement unpredictable and the adventurers you end up with only slightly under your purview. Also the game seems quite punitive in all the wrong ways; tax day shouldn't be more dreaded than adventurers.  Again hugely disappointing for such an awesome theme.

RATING: 8
One of my current favorites. Great theme and components, funny (if somewhat disorganized) rulebook. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Wicked theme, wonderful mechanics, great sense of humor... and very challenging.  After playing with four players and two players I'm really, really impressed with this game.  The teo player game still includes all four player boards, with one order nominally in control of each player, with two random orders per round.  This simulates the other two players pretty darn well, and I did not feel that the game came up lacking at all as a two player game.

Dungeon Lords... ahh Dungeon Lords!

The game has a fantastic theme, and it works really well for me. Like Bryan, I was a fan of the Dungeon Keeper PC games (why the heck has this not come out on Steam or for the Playstation Network already?), but I felt that Vlaada did a great job of capturing the wit and feel of those games.

I love the art. The board, the monsters, the implied douchey-ness of the adventurers. The components all look fantastic (and I have yet to print the minion stickers for my copy as yet!).

Now for the difficult part: explaining the love of the game part of the game. I really enjoy the fact that you need to play the game a bit to get good at it. I think that you need to plan further ahead in this game than in many other games. One turn ahead is not enough. The game tells you what is coming up, there is no way that you should be caught unawares during payday or tax season once you have a couple of games under your belt. I think that you should not only be planning ahead for what you need for a given turn (gold/food/evil) but be trying to mess with the plans of your opponents. I can't understate the importance and fun of using the subtle mechanisms of the game to mess with your opponents. It can be as overt as blocking someone from getting a resource when they need it, bumping them up or down a level in the order resolution for a given order (it can be a terrible blow to be bumped up or down in the order resolution queue for many of the orders, as you may not have the resources to get what you needed from the order, despite ending up with the 'better' position in the queue) or manipulating your evilometer to force a 'bad' adventurer onto an opponent. Take a monster that your opponent wanted, and force one with a more punishing upkeep onto them, or even have them not recruit at all! I just love seeing these little chips in the wall come together and form large cracks. I don't think that you actually need to be punching someone in the face for them to feel like that's what happened at the end of the game!  Even better, once you're playing with people for a while, the metagame starts to become a bigger part of the game, and you're outwitting the plans laid to mess with you!!

Once all that has played out, you get to have fun with a puzzle to defeat the adventurers with what you have at hand! The adventurers you draw are important, but the ability to think on your feet and set up crazy chains of events and wacky situations to win is even more important! Positioning yourself to take and destroy an injured paladin here is also a LOT of fun!

So to sum it up: it's a game with a lot of subtle interaction, that rewards the ability to think on your feet, adjust to an ever changing situation and looks fantastic. What's not to like?

I want to comment (again) about the opinions I've seen that there are not enough meaningful decisions. I agree that at the start you end up with "I need gold, so I'll mine gold" situations. The more you play, you prevent getting into that position, or get screwed HARD by someone and learn the lesson... plan ahead. What I see a lot of new players missing is the fact that the mistake was not necessarily that they didn't have enough gold, but maybe that they did not have enough imps and didn't mine up some extra gold the time the mined prior to that.... missing that the 'bad' decision was actually an imp shortage NOT a gold shortage. The result was not enough gold and a dead letter or three for your trouble.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Very engaging first series of plays! This game reminds me of a twisted version of "Kingsburg," but here the bad guys take turns building up their dungeons for the end of the year when the good guys come - instead of the other way around. Lots of tongue in cheek references! Also very reminiscent of "Dungeon Keeper" the PC game (undoubtedly an influence). Players compete to see who can imprison more "good guys" while preserving as much as possible of what they have built. In what I consider a sop to political correctness, or a way to persuade some that this can be an innocent family game, heroes are "imprisoned," not killed, and the baddies merely "knocked out" for the rest of the season. Ok, sure...

The game consists of two complete years of four seasons each, with players getting three actions per season (from a menu of eight). Thus, twenty-four actions each per game and every one counts! Actions for each season are chosen secretly and revealed simultaneously then resolved in order. Each action "target" can only accommodate three players each season, so it is possible to find you've been shut out of one or more of your precious allotment of actions. Another cool feature is the "Evil Meter" which tracks your "reputation." The more evil you get raises the possibility of attracting the tougher heroes who all score the same in the end (with some exceptions) so managing this is another important aspect of game play. Combat is engaging and surprisingly deep while being familiar at the same time. Oh yes, and you need to make sure you can feed your monsters, and pay taxes on the dungeon you've built as well. Nevertheless, the game plays smoothly and relatively quickly for a game that has a lot going on in it.

On the negative side, the quite fiddly scoring (vaguely reminiscent of Agricola) at the end would have been greatly aided by score sheets that the game chose not to provide. I expect a fan generated score sheet to be one of the first files to be uploaded. That aside, I was very favorably impressed. Rating score is preliminary, stay tuned...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
To early to really give this a 'real' rating, either it's a 8 (or a 9) or a 3 or a 4! For now I'm going with 7.

What I like - the theme, really seems to work for me at the moment.

What I don't like: Blind action - this is a lottery, guess right and be in the right place in the turn order and you do well. Some people like this sort of thing, I don't (much).

Distribution of heroes - it's (relatively) easy to put yourself as 'most evil' and with the right game play the least - but in the middle its another lottery as to your position. Meaning you might get that priest you really don't want, or you might not ...

I think it only really works with 4, currently not a problem as everyone wants to try it. However once the cult of the new move on to the next shiny release only working for 4 could be a major reason for it not getting played.

And it feels like a missed opportunity too - quite fiddly for actual 'game' - and I'd have much prefered a brain controlling the adventurers raiding your dungeon, say having 2 parts of the game. One where you build a dungeon and another where you build a team of dungeon raiders to go raid an opponent's dungeon.

So as it stands, mixed feelings - really need to play it a bit more to decide if all the minuses outweigh the currently 'it feels so cool' theme.

* Update, played again and I was a little surprised to find the shine was off this totally. I just found it a mix of dull and annoying - I don't really get why this needs a blind selection mechanic at all.

** Update, now have a copy, so I'll get a chance to play 2 and 3 player games too, rating might change, or it will hit the trade pile!

** Update - now tried it with 2 and 3 and I've changed my mind, it works fine with both. Still wish blind action selection wasn't the main mechanic, but it's seeing play, and not on the trade pile (for the moment), rating increased to 6

RATING: 7.5
There's a lot to like here but the confusing layout of the rules rather inhibit you from striking up a quick acquaintance - trying to pick up and play took much longer than it should for a game which isn't really that complex mechanically - even if the number of options it offers, combined with the limited number of actions available, means that it is far from straightforward and presents some interesting and difficult decisions.

On first play I liked most of this quite a lot - the theming, approach and many of the detailed game mechanisms are attractive and work well - but I have a nagging doubt over the action choice system.  Actions are the major decision points in the game and you have a very limited number of them - the fact that you can be shut out of even one action seems too significant a factor in a game of this length and it can happen too many times for my taste - not quite at random but at times that is the case.  All part of the game perhaps but it is something which could and should have been done better.

Second and third plays confirm those impressions - a fun game, yes, but with a little too much randomness for the length/weight for my taste.  The variability of the spells is a particular feature I didn't spot first time out - oh how I laughed when my dragon was turned into a sheep - all very well if it hadn't wiped out the results of two or three rounds of planning which set me to buy it and another couple of rounds planning how best to use it offensively - all for naught!  So, play and enjoy - but don't try to take it seriously.

RATING: 5
I've tried to like Dungeon Lords, but 4 plays in, I have to say it just doesn't do much for me. The theme doesn't get traction for me, the pacing seems off, and it seems like there is just too much stuff going on for not enough payoff. It needed to be streamlined, cleaned up, something - some edges filed down (like the really awkward Ghosts) would be a good start, but also maybe narrowing the resources, or accomplishing more with action cards and less with game mechanisms - something. The game as it is, to my mind, is just too unwieldy and not thematically interesting enough. It needed more efficient, more organic theme and better pacing and story development.

This a game that's in a very tough space, fairly abstract resource management, in which there is just a lot of stuff I'd rather play. Dungeon Lords is distinct enough mechanically to justify occasional play, but unfortunately its significant complexity problems militate against this. Galaxy Trucker isn't all that similar a game, but it pushes a lot of the same buttons for me, and is just so much more fun. And the humor in Galaxy Trucker is so much more effective and endearing; trying to use basically the same schtick in Space Alert and Dungeon Lords has become tiresome, especially given the humor has gone from a quirky sidelight to the game that enhanced the ambiance to being overbearing enough to be an impediment to actually playing it.

Given this theme - essentially, that of bad D&D - I think both Cutthroat Caverns and Thunderstone are much more evocative and fun, as well as being far better-paced and more exciting. Dungeon Lords is just an average euro with a dungeon-building veneer. OK, but I think it's getting hit in my opinion because this is such great thematic concept, a game that actually embraced it and made it its own could be really fun. But Dungeon Lords seems like just another euro to me.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
While I wasn't sure the game length was worth the payout after my first play, the game seemed to be a little quicker the second time around, absolving that fear.  However, it's still a game with enough rules to where it's not an easy game just to bring out and recall all the rules to.

One thing I dislike is simultaneous action selection in general.  Specifically, I've always disliked when everyone picks their actions and reveals at the same time.  It just feels like a crap shoot to me, which can stink in a game where so much is riding on every action.  The game gives you a little information at least, since each player has two face up cards which they can't play per turn.  This made it much better for me than typical simultaneous action selection games, but I still don't all that much care for the mechanic.

Complaints aside, building up your dungeon and protecting it from the pesky humans is still a fun exercise, and this theme is really what makes the game shine!  It's somewhat like Galaxy Trucker where you build up your ship and hope it survives.  Here, you build your dungeon and do your best to equip it in fighting off the bands of heroes.

Overall, I'd say this is a decent game to me, but I'd have to be in the right mood to play it.  I can see both fans of the euro and fans of more thematic games getting along on this one as well, which seems few and far between in games!  These Czech guys really know how to make 'em!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Updated 5/13/10.

I've only played the full game once, so all I can say at this point is:

1. The art and components are top-notch. 
2. Mechanics from all of Vlaada's games make appearances, so if you love his work, you'll probably enjoy this one.
3. Going through the rulebook, although a 2-hour game itself, was pretty awesome.  
4. Holy hell, there's a lot of stuff going on here. 

It seems like it should be an awesome combination of some of my favorite mechanics.  In practice, I couldn't juggle that many balls and ended up not having much of a plan at all (does "Buy a bunch of shit and throw it all at the adventuring party" count?).  The action selection phase is a simple risk/reward proposition, but the amount of risk undermines a lot of the ability to plan.  Similarly, while the practice battles all have a neat, optimal solution, the actual ones rarely do.  In all likelihood, it's just a game that needs practice. 

One big drawback of the game is the amount of downtime spent on maintenance.  It just feels unnecessarily fiddly.  Each turn, you make a quick decision (play three cards from your hand), then spend 5 minutes resolving them: Each player, in turn, announces the first unannounced card and places a little worker man.  Once all the worker men are placed, each space on the board is resolved by giving the worker man back along with some pre-determined benefit.  There has to be a more elegant way to accomplish the same thing.     

By reusing mechanics, I do feel like Vlaada's competing against himself a little bit.  I generally prefer Space Alert for programming, and Galaxy Trucker is faster and more chaotic for the build-up-tear-down gameplay, and Through the Ages is just an over-all better game if I'm going to spend this length of time on something.  I would need to be in the right mood to want to play this over the others.  

That last sentence sounds like a 6 to me.  Which seems right (similar to Pandemic; Race for the Galaxy). 

2/1/11:  I had played twice previously, with 2 and with 3, and found it tedious. My negative reaction to it strengthened as it shot up the BGG rankings shortly thereafter, irrationally praised as being "thematic" by people who like funny pictures of fantasy creatures and sculpted plastic bits. I played it again yesterday for the first time in roughly a year (this time with 4), and I found myself enjoying it a fair bit (despite still being somewhat tedious). It's certainly something that I could see myself wanting to play again. It now sits at #35 overall for me, one of my highest 6s.

RATING: 7
Rated 8 after one play. I've played a number of "worker placement" games before trying this one, so my enthusiasm and the novelty of this game is really a reaction to all the other fun and challenging things this game throws at you. I really enjoyed balancing all the various resources, the puzzle of defeating the "good guys" with the right traps and monsters, and the clever rotation of available actions. 

From what I've seen, most Chvatil games are very unique, but titles like this one, Galaxy Trucker, and Space Alert share a few attributes: 1) You need to learn to tolerate luck and uncertainty, but there's often features that allow you to peek into the hidden information, 2) what you build can be torn apart and you will enjoy it, and 3) great themes reinforce game mechanics.

Dungeon Lords has one of the best themes of any game I've played. I've tried a number of different Euros where corn/cloth/ore/spices/blah/blah/whatever eventually devolved into grabbing "gray cubes" and "red cubes." Not so with this one. Gold, food, monster and traps... these things are very memorable simply because the engaging theme reinforces the relationships.

I need to try this again, as the first game is certainly a learning game.

RATING: 10
The personal favourite of my private collection. Theme and gameplay are a blast!
This game has seen a lot of play in our group and 90-120 minutes are a realistic timeframe for a round with an experienced troupe. If you have new players get ready for mor like four hours.Its hard to learn and, even if you know the game, well it can be very very hard and oh boy, can that game be punishing!
Player interaction is limited, but observing your opponent's gameplay is tantamount to success. You always have to be aware of their needs as the other's command, and choice of monsters, chambers, have direct impact on what your choice of action should be.
With new players allways keep in mind to prepare them of what they will have to face in this game! Its one that you must play over and over again before you really get a feeling on how your choices will affect your success or demise.
Start with the easy version, play the full version only if you have nothing to loose in life, and if you really want to know pain (or if you just want more player interaction) use the "minions with gifts" expansion. (The chips for that are in the Core Game and the rules can be downloadet at their homepage)
Players need a thick hide in this game. If, for example, you have a bad first year where the heroes conquer your whole dungeon, you might have the whole second half of the game in front of you without a chance to catch up with other players until the final rating and ranking. In a case like this, stop playing to win! Play for the sake of a good time with your friends, or that cafeteria for your witches and trolls. A themed dungeon awards extra VP after all. Then give those second year heroes a good thrashing to regain at least some of your glory!
In addition each dungeon lord has their presonal goal of scoring more than 0 points in the end. You want to keep your DungeonKeeper-License, Lord, dont' you?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Dungeon Lords is another game in the genre of "you build it and the game tries to destroy it" that I've enjoyed to varying degrees, with Galaxy Trucker probably being the best example in recent memory.

Dungeon Lords uses a programmed, blind action selection mechanism where players select the actions they want to take, and then move their worker pawns out onto the central board where they can get things like monsters, new rooms, traps, and the like. The trick is that being second is better than first at most things, and being fourth means you get nothing. Doing this, you collect dungeon pieces and, at times, evil points which are essentially a way to punish someone for getting better stuff in the collecting stuff phase. Being too evil is very bad because the more dangerous adventurers are drawn to the dungeons of those who were most evil over the course of the game. After each sequence of 4 rounds, the aforementioned adventurers come calling and try to trash the place, and you get to throw monsters and traps at them, hopefully killing them in the process. You do this twice and hopefully have the best dungeon standing at the end of the game.

I enjoyed my play of the game, but I think it's a bit a cumbersome for what it actually offers at the end of the day. My favourite part of this is the same thing that is my favourite part of Galaxy Trucker: seeing my place destroyed or survive. The problem is that this game takes significantly longer. Though I certainly like the theme, I don't think this offers enough to justify regular play on my part. Play on the part of experienced folks would probably drop playtime, which would increase my enjoyment, but I don't think this will be ever something that I suggest myself, mainly because I don't care for blind simultaneous action selection as a central mechanic in games that are longer than 15 minutes. If this isn't a problem for you and you like the theme of the game, then your mileage may vary significantly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I adore the theme.  I find the gameplay tedious.  There are too many arbitrary limits on what can be done, how, by whom, when... but worst of all, again, we have a highly logical, deep planning game totally sabotaged by random elements that cannot be controlled.  If a game is to be entirely decided by randomness, it needs to be very short and cute, not give the appearance of complexity and meaning.

Played with a light heart, talking smack about the theme and not really caring how it turns out, you can have a fun time with this.  Just don't try to win too hard... you'll only be disappointed.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
Dungeon Lords is a big game with a lot of thinking behind it but it's not my cup of tea for some reasons.

1) It plays slow and scripted
There is too much cube-pushing. Placing the minions and resolving the actions is slow and clunky 
Long solo parts like the puzzle-solving attacks to you dungeon are annoying.
The game is divided in hundreds of phases but this do not encode "game", it is all accountancy. A progress bar tells me what to do all the time. Feels like playing a video game (like Dungeon Keeper) being the computer and not the players.

2) Lack of player interaction
In Dungeon Lords you are in serious difficulties for surviving, the game itself is like a giant machine that will snap your fingers if you don't put enough attention.
It has several mechanisms to screw you up when you are too powerful (like taxes, like payday, like paladins) and this mechanisms force similar strategies to all players (like digging after taxes, recruiting monsters after payday)
So, given that any player interaction is done through the blind minion placement and that the game is difficult enough by itself, there is very little chance that you can play "against" you opponents instead of just trying to do "something" quick and useful for you.
The reverse resolution sequence of some actions like monsters and rooms adds only confusion and frustration to the strategy.

3) Scoring
The final score system is just indecent. And slow

4) Art
The drawings on the board are nice, unfortunately they are covered by wooden bits all the time and you don't see em. The drawings on the board are individually nice but all together form a complex and amorph mass of colors that makes me sick. The spaces on the board have no clear delimitation and the pictures have so much detail that need a close inspection to decide what's in every space and what's it for.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Dungeon Lords suffers in my opinion from two problems. The first is that the game creates this [i]hugely complex[/i] dungeon attack and defense mechanism which subsequently gets executed [i]twice[/i] in the entire game. I consider it inexcusable that so much game real estate is effectively wasted on a mechanism which is only seldomly used. If there had been more attacks on the dungeon the rules burden for this aspect would have been a lot less problematic, I think. The second problem is that Vlaada appears to consider it a good idea to play for silly goals which detract from the actual process of [i]winning[/i]. He violates Knizia's tenet that the goal of a game is to win, but that it is the goal that is important, and not the winning. It may appeal to people who play 'just because' and, indeed, 'just want to build a great dungeon', but really, purchase a set of LEGO bricks, and construct your own dungeon if that is so important to you. Dungeon Lords is marketed as a [i]game[/i], and under those circumstances the rather weak end scoring makes it difficult to treat this title as a game worth playing—i.e., with winning in mind.

That said, the worker placement mechanicsm which ties everything together is well-crafted and can be played without nearly a word of explanation thanks to the clear iconography. But since it all comes together again in just two attack waves of a bunch of goody-gooders, I didn't feel particularly challenged to do my best here. Put another way: if I want to play a construction puzzle I'll put out something like Shipyard or 20th Century which make the puzzle element the core of the game; and if I want to play a worker placement game, I'll pick something which emphasises that aspect. (Can't really say what at the moment; I rarely seem to enjoy WP games.) Dungeon Lords is [i]vlees noch vis[/i] as I would say in Dutch: a saying meaning, literally, that the game is neither meat nor fish, in order words, lacking substantial and unique nourishing qualities. I fear this is a characteristic of many of Vlaada's games: the ideas swirling around in that brain of his are wonderful, but they do not always lead to good [i]games[/i]... and that's a shame. He should consider teaming up with a designer who is capable of creating good games. And then wait quietly for his bank statement to hit seven or more numbers.

RATING: 6
Components: Gorgeous components, doing much to aid in the thematic immersion. I appreciate the minor details that differentiate the various adventurers. The iconography is clear, despite so many different functions being communicated. The imps are great.

Experience: First game, two players, clocked in at 4.5 hours. Wow; that's epic (that includes rules and the four training scenarios). There seems to be a disjoint between the order resolution and the combat phase. So much of the game hinges on what happens during combat, and there is such a puzzle element to defeating the encroaching adventurers efficiently, that the game might be better served to minimize the amount of time spent outside of the combat phase (e.g. by simplifying the order resolution). Very eager to try again. With those nasty do-gooder Paladins romping about it doesn't pay to be evil! (The scoring is disheartening; to spend so long on a game, and only earn four points as my reward.)

Second game, a slim 2 hours. Absolutely brutal. This is an unforgiving game. There were several order phases where I miscalculated and ended up either taking zero actions with my minions, or actually being a bit worse off because of my actions (e.g. investing my resources in a monster, who I then was unable to feed come payday). Unlike in many other games, my mistakes do not necessarily benefit my opponent. There is little we can do during the orders phase to adjust to miscalculations, and the combat phase is just an all-out brawl with the adventurers, throwing everything we've got at them to keep them out of our precious dungeon. This game is very difficult to play.

Third game. Just too brutal. I shoot myself in the foot, and my foot shoots back. My mistakes are hard to foresee, and even more difficult to correct. The combat phase is so much more fun than the worker placement phase; I'd much prefer to have a game that focused mostly on the combat. I don't think I'll be playing this again.

First Played: 2011-03-26
Last Played: 2012-05-19
Eagerness to Play Again: Eventually

Plays (3):
2 players: 2
3 players: 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A very good "indirect" euro style game.  

This game also combines elements from many great games like puerto rico and settlers of catan, but adds in a dramatic twist to goal choosing.  Quite frankly the path to victory is not clearly defined and it takes quite a bit of thought to figure out how to overcome the challenges before you, but that is the beauty of the design.  

For example, instead of drawing a finish line for a the players and telling them to run, the designer provided a framework of paths, many of which simply will not work every game.  This helps overcome the issues associated with players figuring out the "path of least resistance" and using that strategy every game.  

Fun game, but can be quite challenging for some players.  "Euro gamers" find it difficult to figure out what to do since the path to victory isn't laid down in stone, while many "old school" strategy gamers seem to do just fine.  

EDIT:  After playing this game for about 8 months I'm downgrading my rating from 9.2 to 7.5

There is strategy involved in this game, but this game is ruthlessly random.  

You can run and attempt strategies.  For example, picking up smaller creatures and supplementing with traps works.  The problem is that the events are very harsh (time and time again) and the limited resources almost always push a player out of what they need.  

What this does is prevents players from getting ahead with skill, and puts quite alot of game into the realm of luck.  IMO, some luck is a good thing, too much ruins it.

RATING: 5.5
There are a bunch of neat ideas in this game, and the components are pretty cool.  I enjoy the math-puzzle nature of the adventurer attacks.  However, the blind action selection mechanism is a tragedy.  It reduces the game's only direct player interaction to simple guesswork, and the way the order of the blind selections affects the costs and benefits of each action is just weird (and allows for a bad guess or two to really hurt one's position long-term).  In a simpler game it wouldn't be so bad, but this one requires a fair amount of mental effort -- too much for the random screwage of the action selection to be fun.

I'm interested in trying the game with open action selection, because I do like a lot of what it offers.  We'll see.

RATING: 7
[2016-05-23 Rating: 6 -> 7]
After playing Dungeon Lords again, I have to up my rating a little bit. Once I got all the small rules details down and could come up with kind of a plan, the game became more enjoyable.

[2009-11-02 Rating: n.a. -> 6]
Summary:
---------
In Dungeon Lords, you are a dungeon lord trying to build the best dungeon out there. To achieve that, you are given the timespan of two years. Each year is divided into two distinct phases.

First Phase - Resource Collection: 
You have three workers that you can assign to different tasks like hiring some monsters, getting little helpers called imps or plundering a village for some food. How evil you are is determined by the actions you choose.
The mechanism to select your actions reminds me very much of one of my favorite games "Wallenstein": All players select the three tasks they want to perform simultaneously by playing three action-cards face down on their player-mat. After everyone has finished their selection, the workers will be assigned. Note that it is possible, that the spot you wanted to have isn't available to you anymore because the other players already took it. This phase is repeated four times.

Second Phase - Defend your dungeon:
Based on your decisions in the first phase, you are assigned some heroes that now enter your dungeon and you try to defend them with the traps and monsters you purchased in the first phase.
Basically, this is a puzzle each player has to solve on their own.

After two years the game is over and you are awarded points for your remaining dungeon and the heroes you could imprison during the second phase. In addition, there are some titles like "Evil lord with most dungeon tiles" which will get you more points.

The player with the most points is declared the winner.

Comments:
--------------
* Dungeon Lords is a quite fiddly game. You have to remember a lot of small things in order to play the game (well). In this regard it reminded me of the solo-game of Race for the Galaxy: There is so much book-keeping that I had to do that I just wished, this was a computer-implementation. I wouldn't call this game elegant in any way.

* There's isn't too much player-interaction. You can try to annoy the other players by taking what you think they want, but you can't really plan this.

* At it's core, this is a worker-placement-game and in this regard it has too much luck (spells, available monsters and traps) for my liking.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
A great theme but one which is not really engaged with by the game. 

- The main mechanic is worker placement, perhaps not the main job criteria you would associate with an evil overlord - 'Dungeon Lords: The HR department'.
- The dungeons you build are typically not much more than a few corridors and a couple of rooms - hardly a labyrinth of despair.
- The combat portion of the game is an AP prone puzzle and, though quite interesting and challenging, does not conjure up the high drama of you marshalling your hordes to overwhelm the adventurers.
- The game also runs too long and has too much fiddly rules overhead.
- 'Fun' is in short supply.

Also, the worker placement element is quite tight, which I rarely like in worker placement/action selection games. You can get completely screwed by not being able to get to a particular placement slot, to the extent of your game being almost over at the half-way stage. Although you can second guess what opponents may do to try and mitigate the risks of placement, you can sometimes just get unlucky as to which 50-50's other players choose to go for. 

One of the more over-rated games on BGG.

SOLD - a clever, if fiddly game, but somewhat dry and I would never suggest it for play and it is too complex for play at home.

RATING: 9
Dungeon Lords is a game where each player is fighting the mechanics of the game (the good and noble heroes). It has building element similar to galaxy trucker (and is the same designer as galaxy trucker). It has a real sense of anticipation and it is less complicated and galaxy trucker. Each individual is focusing on themselves but you are always looking over the shoulder of the other player anticipating what they are going to do as it will affect your board as well. This element is key to sharing and involved game with your friends. The board is beautiful and there are many pieces but once you learn the concept of the game it is fairly easy to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players are evil lords trying to get their Dungeon Lord license.  Players spend four seasons (turns) sending out their minions to do their bidding - get food, money, tunnel out a bit of dungeon or convert a dungeon tunnel into a room, etc.  You also have to hire monsters and imps, and purchase traps.  After a year of this stuff, a party of adventurers comes looting in your dungeon, and you enter a combat mini-game.  The adventurers have to be beaten quickly, or they will render good chunks of your construction useless.  Points are tallied after to years of building, and two attacks.  Gorgeous looking game, and it's by Vlaada Chvatil so it was an auto-purchase.  However, it's a strange beast - half resource management, half tactical combat game.  Certainly getting better after 5+ plays.  Excellent rules and components, just have to see if it stays fresh and fun.

RATING: 7

For those who are curious about the differences, Dungeon Petz is worker placement + putting on a show. It's all about maximizing how best to prepare your pets for selling.

Dungeon Lords is action selection/programming + puzzle with a sprinkling of spatial building. It's all about preparing your dungeon to not be trampled by the incoming party.

RATING: 6
I had extremely high hopes for Dungeon Lords but was let down.  I had heard this described as a worker placement game, but it's more of a simultaneous action selection game.  Sometimes you can get blocked out of an action but that tends to happen minimally.  

So what are the caveats?  For this gamer's game, there is a fairly high degree of randomness/chaos.  It's hard to plan to try and hire a specific monster (or ghost) because you may be locked out of the Hiring action the round that this specific Monster (or Ghost) is available.  Also the combat cards have a huge range of effects and amount of fatigue they deliver.  There are some ways to mitigate this but after an initial play it feels like you just have to make the best of what you have.

Another thing that I was disappointed about the number of decisions based on the length of the game.  I didn't feel it was as high of a ratio as I would have liked.  Finally, the box says 2 - 4 players, but I would never consider playing this with less than 4 players.  It would speed up the combat sequence to play with 2 or 3 players but it makes the action selection sequence a lot less enjoyable due to some random programing of a neutral player(s).

Now this may sound as if I don't like the game, however it is enjoyable and that's due to the masterfully written rulebook, wonderful artwork and great theme.  It's a euro through-and-through, but people couldn't claim euros to be themeless if more designers made games like Dungeon Lords.  The theme is so great that this is one of the few euros I can get my ameritrash-loving friends to play.

The best thing I can say, is try this one before you buy it to see if it's right for you.

RATING: 7
The standard CGE humor and atypical theme of Dungeon Lords creates an immersive game for players to explore. The action selection mechanic works to both reward late activations, and punish the last activation (potentially). This, along with the locking of use assignment cards, establishes an element of tactics that deviate enough from the standard worker placement / action selection to keep it from feeling mundane. The combat system contrasts well with the bureaucratic nature of the dungeon building, and is filled with a plethora of adventurers to keep strategies nimble. Combat resolution is wide open, which can slow the game down a bit, but also forces the lords to creatively thwart spells, plant traps, and deploy monsters. Don't let its reputation fool you. Being a tyrannical subterranean imp overlord is not as difficult as the internet would lead you to believe (though the expansions offer a myriad of ways to beef up the experience to your own liking).

RATING: 9
I am a big Dungeon Keeper fan (computer game).  I have played all the scenarios from Dungeon Keeper 1 and Dungeon Keeper II multiple times, and install Dungeon Keeper 2 on every computer I have.  (Keeper, the dungeon floors are bumpy, imps are having a rough time playing marbles).  I have also played Dig'n'Fight click-fest browser-game.  So, bear this in mind; because I really enjoy the genre, and this could influence my rating.

Dungeon Lords is primarily a game where players are vying to protect their dungeons from the marauding heroes.  The crux of the game is to build your dungeon while trying to maneuver your 'evil' score to get the type of hero you may defeat the easiest (with traps / monsters / combination).  The frustration occurs when players 'bump' your choice of action with their choices (4 players, 3 players per action type), and reverse positioning for monsters / rooms becomes important.

I enjoy the game, and find it fairly light (note: I really enjoy 18xx, Le Havre, and Die Macher; so my 'light' score may be skewed).

Rated after 5 plays.

RATING: 9
This game is great fun. If you like worker placement games, aren't afraid of heavy games, and have ever played an RPG, you are going to enjoy this a lot.
The worker placement mechanic was given a great twist in this game: up to 3 workers (out of 4 players) may be placed in each resource spot, and each spot gets an overall better reward than the last one. And since worker placement is chosen secretly and simultaniously, you will have to carefuly choose what you really want (may lead to AP, though).
And then you've got the adventurers trying to destroy your dungeon, which makes for a kind of puzzle game, where you try to use your evil resources optimally to reduce the havoc!
And all the mechanics affect each other, and you will always have the feeling that you can't get everything done, which is great.
Something that might be an issue with this game is how it relies heavily on 4 players, so you are actually playing three different games whether there are 2, 3, or 4 players. Which isn't necessarily bad.

Overall, if you don't mind heavy games, you are in for a treat!

RATING: 3
The idea sounds exciting – you’re a dungeon keeper and you need to protect your place from pesky heroes who keep invading, killing your minions, and taking your stuff. The whimsical artwork seems to support this lighthearted theme. But in practice, you’re playing a tedious worker placement game where simple actions are chopped up into micro-actions and scrambled around so the players can have the ostensible fun of piecing them back together. 

I found the experience uninspiring to say the least. Surprisingly, the focus is more on resource collection than hero fighting. They packed in plenty of accounting but went light on the fighting. The heroes entering the dungeon -- which should be the majority of the game -- happens just twice. I’m not a fan of micro-action games where simple things become extremely complicated. Chopping things up into smaller pieces that must be reassembled doesn’t create depth, it creates tedium. How about a game where you load up your dungeon frequently and fight heroes frequently? Isn’t that why we’re here? This is more like Dungeon Quartermaster. 

For a much better implementation of what I’m driving at, try [thing=173460][/thing] and see how fun it is to assemble your dungeon dudes and have them fight heroes. Oh, those damn Hobbits are annoying! Now, that's how you make someone feel like a dungeon lord.

RATING: 7
Fun for having a two-phase mechanic, resource acquisition worker-placement style, then battle.  Also, the priority and aging of "actions" (3 actions each turn, 2 of which cannot be reused next and are public information) makes for an interesting extra level of planning on which actions you must do before your opponents and which they cannot do.

After only two plays (and with time I may feel otherwise), the game seems marred by many detailed rules that are hard to remember and not often will explained, in an already overly long set of instructions.  For instance, not paying taxes costs damage markers but not paying monsters (same game aspect as taxes) costs reputation for not having food but nothing when the monster costs reputation instead of food and you are out (max evil), but monsters paid for in other monsters and traps cost nothing on payday.  Well, apparently. 

The victory point salad of "titles" for the most tunnels, rooms, imps, etc. seems awkward too as something to strive for.

RATING: 7
This is a game about balancing just how 'evil' you are to get the right mix of hero’s to turn up at the end of the year, too evil and the paladin will show up and he's pretty hard core, not evil enough and you can face a party of all rogues making traps useless or all clerics who heal the party after each monster attack.

You need to get a mixed party of adventurers to be able to knock them out, so you have to do evil deeds to jump up the evil track followed quickly be good deeds to drop you back down the track.  
This balancing act combined with the actual creation of your dungeon paradise, creation of imps, hiring of monsters and acquiring of gold make this a fascinating game.

RATING: 2
Played one time with 3 players.

MAY 2014: As soon as I saw the board, I knew I was in trouble. Thus far, most of the icon-heavy, cube-pushing Euros have left me cold. After a couple turns, I knew I didn't care for this game, but I got a few rules wrong, and didn't have combat explained to me that thoroughly, AND got blindsided by an event card I didn't know existed...and it was all downhill from there. In a best case scenario, with everything explained perfectly, comfort with the mechanics, etc, this *might* hit 5, but probably a 4.

There simply was nothing about this game I liked: crowded graphic design, overly balanced and like-for-like action spaces, rooms that acted similar to actions spaces. Just same old, same old.

User sisteray used a term called "parallel play engine games". If it means what I think it means: EXACTLY THIS. Overly-balanced acquisition games with one unique mechanic strapped on. See: Myrmes, Industrial Waste, etc.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Despite the theme, Dungeon Lords is a classic Euro worker placement game. In order to create the most impressive dungeon you hire monsters, dig tunnels, .. and pay your taxes. And at the end of the year, just when you start to get that feeling of accomplishment a band of heroes stumbles down your rabbit hole and tries to turn your precious halls into a pile of rubble.

During the dungeon building phase you can use your imps to collect resources, extend the dungeon, or hire monsters. Each action, however, can only be executed at most three times per turn (in a game with 4 players) so if everybody wants to do the same thing one will leave emty-handed, and depending on when exactly you choose an action the costs and benefits will vary. Of course, action selection is done secretly and simultaneously so there is quite a bit of bluffing and second-guessing involved as well.

Fighting the adventurers off at the end of the year feels more like a puzzle than anything else, trying to optimise your monsters, traps, and other dungeon features to defeat the party as quickly and with as little damage as possible.

So, worker placement, yes, but not simply copying other games, and with its own humourous angle. Beware the paladin!

RATING: 8

Oddly reminds me of Agricola in that it's a worker placement that drips with theme, there are a lot of scoring categories to keep track of, and there are 'tax' phases that loom over the proceedings. It's probably no harder to teach or learn than Agricola, to boot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Dungeon Lords makes the grave mistake of being complex without being deep.  It is a largely tactical worker placement game with very little interaction (other than checking other peoples position on the evilometer, and groaning when someone slips in ahead of you on a resource space - even this is not overly penalizing).  

The game bogs down with heady computations and number crunching to make sure the best possible trap/monster layout is in line for the upcoming adventurers.  This overly mathematical resolve pulls one away even further from the already loosely veneered theme.

The events just make this difficult for difficult's sake (maybe for a forced laugh - sadistic humor by Vlaada?) feeling more like a write off of actions than an interesting challenge.   

Neither fun or compelling.  Wasted theme and concept.  Insanely long for the amount of decision space.  Too much upkeep, too little game play.

每人选定3张指令牌(每人手上都有相同1套共8张的指令牌，类似银河竞逐)，按顺序摆放好，然后大家一齐全部翻开，按顺序按指令牌指派小鬼到公共操作区霸占位置执行行动，公共操作区每个行动都有3个位置可以霸占，换言之第4个玩家选择同一个行动，则不能干活。按公共操作区，从左到右从上到下执行行动，同一个行动从上到下执行，有些例外是从下到上执行。同一行动上下3行都有不同的优惠，普遍是后执行的比早执行的要更有优惠。行动有：魚肉村民(获得食物)，隱惡揚善(降低恶名)，任意挖掘(开拓自己的地下城)，開採礦藏(获得金钱)，養殖小鬼(获得小鬼，即工人)，安置陷阱(获得陷阱牌，有利于和英雄战斗)，馴養怪物(获得怪物牌，有利于和英雄战斗)，設置密室(兴建特殊功能的房间)。
每年最后一季会有英雄来攻打各自的地下城，英雄分不同的等级，恶名最高的玩家要面对等级最高的英雄，和英雄开战类似三国杀，扣血到零就杀死对方，和英雄开战只进行4回合，如果最后依然有英雄存活，则需要按规则拆掉相应的地下室。
感觉这个游戏没多大新意，感觉就是经典的建筑策略游戏+三国杀。

It seems like it will be a lot of fun when everyone knows what is going on... but getting a group together to sit down for the 45 minutes of time required for the rules explanation alone... before you even begin to play... a 2 hour game... has been impossible.  I host regular game nights, and it has yet to be played there.  It is to the point where one game night the invite will have a sub invite of "who is willing to sit in a room with no distractions for 3 hours to learn this complicated looking game that really isn't complicated?" to guarantee that I get a group interested in playing and to know what they are in for beforehand.  That is a pretty big strike against the game.  If you have four people who know how to play, that obviously isn't an issue, and the game isn't actually that long to play.  But getting to that point...

The board and components are all really really nice and the game is dripping with flavor.  It really seems fun.  Hopefully, one day, I will get to play it with 3 other people who know how to play.

RATING: 9
I'm liking this game a lot. It's kind of a blend between Agricola and Galaxy Trucker with a dungeon theme. It has the build up and looks of Agricola with the destruction of Galaxy Trucker. The game can be a bit confusing and a little difficult to learn at first, but with a few plays things start to come around and strategies start to become more apparent.

The game looks great and is a lot of fun to play. I love the theme and it has a good feel to it. There are a lot of things going on and remembering the order of how things get played is key to developing a good strategy. The components are well made and look good. I found gameplay to be around the 2 1/2 to 3 hour time period, once you get the hang of things. It's a tense game right up till the end and scoring is one of my favorite parts of the game. There are many way to score points and earn title at game end which move players score back and forth. Making for an exciting and unknown ending.

Excellent game that I'll play over Agricola any day. I'm VERY happy with this purchase and am in hopes of seeing some expansions for it.

RATING: 7
Dungeon Lords is a good game with one nasty flaw.  It's a formidable game to teach to new players.  Going through every detail takes too long, but glossing over the finer points can lead to mass confusion.  Then once you've explained how to play, you'll probably have to suffer one very slow and mistake-filled game while the new players learn the system and the basic strategies.  Once everyone knows the game, it's a lot of fun.  But getting to that point requires some patience.  My current thinking is that the best approach is to give a very cursory overview of the game, then launch right into playing, with the clear understanding that the first play will be a kind of interactive tutorial, "but by the end, you'll understand how everything works."

Dungeon Lords and Galaxy Trucker both have a similar two-phase structure.  First, players piece together their thingamajig (dungeon or spaceship), then they watch as the bad guys try to tear it apart.  Do this three times, and then see who did the best job.  But I don't want to oversell the comparison, because the two games have a totally different feel.  Galaxy Trucker is played in real time, while Dungeon Lords is more methodical.  Personally, I enjoy the frantic real-time building of Galaxy Trucker more than the measured action selection of Dungeon Lords.  However, the tear-down phase of Dungeon Lords is more fun, and involves a lot more decision making.

After reading the rules, I thought Dungeon Lords would be strictly a four-player game.  I was wrong.  While four players is the best configuration, the three-player game is fine.  The dummy player workaround is less of a kludge than I expected.  I've never tried it with two, but the number of reports and photos of two-player games is promising.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This game missed the mark. The action selection is a lot of fun, and the planning for combat has its interesting parts, but the game just veers way too heavily toward resource gathering. It has Vlaada's signature of not being able to do much on a turn and just feeling overly constrained. It's very surprising that you only fight two parties during the game. In short, I like several of the mechanics a lot, but the design center and use of theme are off.

RATING: 8.5
Dungeon Lords is a very good game in which you try to build the best dungeon to trapt as many adventurers before they do damage to your dungeon.  To do so, you build tunnels and rooms, buy trapt and hire monster.

It's a game that is quite complicated to understand and teach before your first play.  It's almost two game in one (with the building phase and the combat phase being so much different)
After a game you realize that it's not so complicated and that the progress board is really well done and make things a lot easier.
I really like the combat mechanic.  It's not a dice rolling type of combat.  There is no radomness during combat, except for the spell cards (but you can see them during the building phase).

RATING: 6
Enjoyed this better than the typical farming/building Euro.  It is still fun to watch your board build up with resources, tunnels, rooms, imps, traps and monsters like other games.  Instead of a simple harvest or feeding phase like similar Euros, everyone has to fend off an attack a little like Kingsburg.  However, unlike KingsBurg, instead of a simple one step pass/fail calculation, there is additional strategy in which traps, monsters you play and in which order you play them.  This is the only significant part of the game that is resolved in turn order, so the game doesn't drag terribly.  As such, worker placement is planned with cards each turn at the same time, so there is no waiting for others.  Also enjoyed trying to second guess other players' placements, as you can benefit yourself more if you place your worker at the right time in the rotation. 

I suspect I can leave a higher rating, if I am able to play again and it retains replayability.  Seems ripe for expansion.

RATING: N/A
