=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A convoluted worker placement game that is very much a Vlaada's creation: excessively complex, too long and designed for exactly four players, with scaling down as an afterthought. You first build your dungeon and then struggle to avoid its destruction, reminding me a bit of "Galaxy Trucker". This happens twice in the game, but it might have been better if you could build your dungeon faster and were attacked just once, cutting the playing time in half. I have played it only with two players and it still takes about three hours.

Playing with fewer than four requires the use of dummy players, but at least it gives you a bit more control over the worker placement phase. This can be very chaotic, as you must plan your actions in advance and can be blocked if other players make the same choices. And even if you are not totally blocked, getting the wrong position in an action space (with their different costs and benefits) can still wreck your plans. After four rounds, there is an interesting but complicated combat puzzle where you must account for random card effects.

Despite its flaws, the game is quite enjoyable, definitely helped by its fun theme that puts you in the role of the bad guys. With some streamlining and less randomness, it could have been great.

RATING: 7
I'm still not sure about the game.  It's fairly fun, but it has a bit of a learning curve.  I'd like to see it played out some with  no 'new' players.  Unfortunately, the way my gaming group works, it seems like we are always teaching at least one player at the table.

There are a lot of rules there and takes about 20-30 minutes to really teach.  The game itself is easy to understand I think (although I've had a player that just couldn't get it and was forgetting things I taught them).  But, over all I think it's intuitive.  BUT, there is A LOT that NEEDS to get done and only a few actions each turn.  It's very easy not to get done, what you need to do and fall behind.  The game seems to be very 'unforgiving' in that manner.  Because of that I do think that with the learning curve it's hard (not impossible) for 'inexperienced' players to compete.  Which can be frustrating for some.

I find it an interesting game though and wouldn't turn down a game, but I don't find myself craving to play it.

RATING: 8
2-5-10
I liked this game quite a bit despite the long time it takes to learn.  Once it clicks though, it does flow quite smoothly.  I had a lot of fun my first play until the scoring came in.  It feels like the game would be less fun if I just focused on maximizing points, and the theme would diminish from a dungeon defending game to an efficiency type game.

I also don't like how the computer player in a 3-player game works.  It's so random that it can be hard to judge when to play certain action cards.  I screwed myself out of a critical card due to this and while I could have tried to foresee the computer playing that card before me, there was no way to know.  I think this would be remedied in a 4-player game where you can at least try to analyze what the other players might do at what times and ignore the complete arbitrary randomness.

I'd like to rate this higher as I do really like the theme and the gameplay once you learn it, but the scoring system seems to contradict building a big dungeon with big baddies and traps.  A small, efficient dungeon which was rewarded for having the least destruction and most assets ended up winning.  I'll have to play it again and see how strongly the scoring factors into my enjoyment, but it really bothered me after the game.  Unfortunate because I liked everything else!

RATING: 2
I've read that one's initial impressions of a game are not only based on the game itself, but within the context of how it was presented/taught and what the outcome was for the player in question. I agree.

My first and last experience of this game is based on that. The rules were glossed over with what I thought was a poor explanation, and there was really no context given as to what I should be doing to do well at this game. Some questions were rebuffed by "don't worry about that for this game" and the like. Clearly, there were a bunch of rules that were too complicated to go over for this introductory session, but in the end, I felt like I was abandoned to fend for myself resulting in a ridiculously negative score. Yes, the gaming session was one of the worst I had ever experienced, so much so that I thought about quitting the hobby altogether.

So, what about the game? On the surface, it seems to have a blend of novel mechanics. The worker placement coupled with the limited action-card selection is neat: there is a limited queue where action-cards are loaded, and these are executed in turn-order to determine how the workers are placed. An action you take now will not be available the next turn, unless you queue it in under specific conditions. Layout of the dungeon tiles seems neat at first, but quickly becomes a dry affair--you're trying to set up your dungeon to "defend" against the human adventurers, but how they wander through the dungeon is based on strict rules; so you are not arranging them to form like an adventure, but setting up layers of defense, as they will destroy one or several rooms per turn. There is a unique "evilness" meter that measures how dastardly you are being, which allows collection of more resources and what not. It is very similar to collecting corruption in Cleopatra, waking up your apprentices too early in Fresco, or Forced Labor in Antiquity; you'll get more done, but if your evilness reaches the bottom of the track, this attracts the most powerful human adventurer known as the Paladin--and he'll do pretty serious damage to your dungeon, so you don't want your evilness to get out of hand. There is a need to balance food production and worker production, and trying to expand your dungeon, while keeping everyone fed is punitive and annoying. The most annoying aspect of the game is how the human adventurers are handled as they descend into your dungeon. It is based on a linear series of conditionals, starting with traps and poisons, monsters, and ending with magic, which feels more like a process flow-chart and puzzle-solving than it feels like running a dungeon. If some of the humans survive through this gauntlet (and combat is simplified, like in Arkham Horror), then they do some damage to your dungeon.

All in all, nice components and visually appealing. However, the gameplay is clunky, unintuitive, punitive, and linear. I had imagined that this game would recapture the feel of being a dungeon master back in my RPG days, but it is not. It is more about the administrative aspects of building/expanding/maintaining your dungeon--all not fun. And those pesky humans are always trying to mess it up. If it were not for the horrible experience I had the first time, I may be inclined to give this a '5' as there is probably a game in there somewhere. However, with the latent, abysmal feel of the game, coupled with a bad experience, I know I will never play this again, and hence my '2'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
One of the better programmed action games with a novel 'dungeon exploring in reverse' theme. There's a strange inverse snowball effect at work: by remaining virtuous you attract the wimpier adventurers to your domain and then you need to spend fewer resources on traps and monsters to keep them out. This leads me to suspect that there's less replay value here than meets the eye. I won my first game of this quite comfortably against more experienced opponents and that's always a danger sign in my book. I enjoyed it and I'll play again, but this may lose its shine after a few plays.

RATING: 7
Can't argue with the production quality, and of course the theme is very appealing.  This is a programming game, kind of like Shogun, but with a worker-placement cast to it, which in practice means it's pretty easy to think things out carefully and still find yourself thwarted.  Once you resign yourself to that, it is pretty fun to build up the various parts of your dungeon and plan out your fights with the adventurers.  The fights go on different lengths of time for different players, which can create more downtime.  

I'd like to try the advanced game.  For me, this is a good once-in-a-while change of pace game. (3 plays)

RATING: 8
Being a Dungeon Administrator is a real pain in the ass.  First, there is the careful management and distribution of resources such as gold, food and imps, the artful methods of hiring monsters and spying on the fighters guild, and the careful crafting of dungeon corridors, rooms and traps.
Then once you have everything meticulously planed out, a group of heroes comes through and destroys everything you worked so hard to build.
Despite the frustration of watching your dungeon waste away to ruins, this is actually a really fun game.

[u]UPDATE:[/u]  Okay as you can see from my pre-playing comments above, luck was the biggest concern for me. I've only played once but I think the level is close to okay, maybe a little more than I might like but ah hell, maybe I am being a little soft due to the great theme but its not that bad really. 

Well themed and intergrated as usual for Vlaada and beautifully produced as well as we can also except from this great company. Take some crisis management and mix in slightly overblown resource management, use the GT platform (build to be destroyed) and reduce the randomness somewhat and you have DL, sort of. Programmed actions drive your game and you can get slightly burned here but if you are paying attention to the others you normally wont, good planning pays off too.  

Though the rule explaination was involved and long the game flowed very well and flew by for our group, first game was only 2 hours, I suspect that could be brought down to 90 minutes with a fast group, might stay at 2 for an average group. I see people saying deep and I see people saying fiddly, we never found it that fiddly really and not really deep either, some but it is good and meaty at any rate. Planning is necessary for sure but you wont know all the perils coming your way so you just do your best, has that Euro game quality where you need everything and wont get it. 

I think I would say just as a summary statement if you want a much meatier and somewhat less random game than Galaxy Trucker (no tile grab to build however) in around 2 hours, you shouldn't be disappointed. A bit of a fine line here but that is exactly what I expected and overall I enjoyed it in my one play and I could see 4 more for sure. As you might expect the fun level is somewhat lower (IMO) since this is more of a serious game, but so is the luck so that is the trade-off. Luck stung me a couple of times but it got the others too and the effects weren't too big, so far I'm okay with it but between a group of competitive serious gamers (my normal group which this first game was not with) you/I might think its a little high, I'll need to play again to judge it for certain. Of course liking the theme will help for those into Fantasy or Dungeons, I never played the PC game Dungeonkeeper to my memory but I am a dungeon crawler so it probably won an extra point here from me though I try to refrain from those things in my ratings. Non-fantasy guys, well, just another resource management game, maybe, but still a good one I think. Expectations and your like of the theme will put this between a 6 - 8 for a pretty big group of people.

Initial Rating: 8.0 (September 2010)

The first Vlaada game I played (I believe) and it IS a great game.

I know the rules are hard to understand at first (if you only read the hilarious rulebook) but if you show one turn of play using the great boardgame graphic design as a teaching aid, it is very easy and intuitive to play.

I love the theme and worker placement actions are very tight in this game (as you only get 3 actions per turn), perhaps my only misgiving is that the score system is not clearly related to what you are doing in the game, this has allowed me to win games even though I have the most conquered dungeon. 

Dungeon Lords is complex enough that it pleases my inner gamer, but it is also lots of fun, the fight against justice is really cool to watch and it is very funny to see how the heroes destroy your opponents´ dungeons.

Bottom line, Dungeon Lords is a game I want to own and one I will explore further in the near future.

Current Rating: 8.0

2014-06-17

With some many games clamoring attention, I haven’t played Dungeon Lords as much as I wanted to, so it’s been a long time since I last played it :(, however I still regard it as a great game and I won’t say no to a play of Dungeon Lords when the opportunity arise. 

For now, I still think this is still better than its thematic descendant Dungeon Petz, but both games are very good in my book (Dungeon Petz also made my 2011 top ten favorite games list).

In Dungeon Lords, the theme oozes everywhere and that’s weird and exciting to have in an euro game. The game is very tight with lots of hard/interesting decisions to make every turn.

On the bad side (for some), it can be a bit harder to teach as it has many rules, also its play time can be on the long side (but that doesn’t bother me).

In conclusion, Dungeon Lords is a great game that I want to play often. It is also the game who opened my eyes toward one of the best game designers out there (perhaps my favorite). To this day, I will buy anything Vlaada puts out with my eyes closed.

RATING: 7
Expectations were sky-high for Dungeon Lords.  And it mostly delivers.  Mostly.

Worker placement's been done to death but mostly as a queue, not a stack.  I like that latecomers get better versions of the actions.  The simultaneous action selection in DL doesn't exactly seem like worker placement to me.  Which is fine - the world doesn't really need a fantasy-themed Caylus.

The combat is engaging.  The game flow is complex at first but not actually that difficult to assimilate.

Obviously theme is not going to be an issue.

I think the reason why I only like, not love, Dungeon Lords is that the resources are very tight, which probably makes the game better mechanically but creates thematic dissonance for me.  I rule a dungeon and I can't scrape together enough food to feed my troll?  Running a dungeon feels a lot like running a business.  Perhaps that's realistic but realism doesn't appear to be the object here.

In the end I'm not sure I have more than 7-10 plays of Dungeon Lords in me over the next year or two.  If I have more it will be on the strength of the theme.  Still worth the purchase, as this will be a fun game to bring back out when my son is older.

RATING: 7

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Be a Dungeon Lord, in the game Dungeon Lords, lording it up and building dungeons! The game appears to be inspired by the old computer game, Dungeon Keeper, a real-time strategy game where players used little minions to gather resources, build rooms and halls, and attract the best monsters to move in. Eventually adventurers would come around and start knocking heads and as master of the dungeon, your job was to stop them. Exact same idea here.

The game has a simple structure: you spend 4 turns building your dungeon, then you fend off invading adventurers with traps and monsters, then you spend 4 more turns building your dungeon, then you again fend off an adventuring party, and the game ends. The game is difficult to teach because you pretty much have to learn it backwards. You have no idea what to build into your dungeon until you know what happens when the adventurers arrive. They tried to make the rulebook follow this structure: teach combat, then describe the rules to the game, then describe combat again. It makes the rules a bit long and cumbersome and I had difficulty getting through them with all the jumping around. It reads like more of a training manual than a rulebook and I struggled with the non-linear nature of it. I just don't have much patience with all that hand-holding. But there is excellent dungeon humor sprinkled throughout. Funniest rulebook I've ever read.

Each player has their own personal dungeon board with a grid to put hallways and rooms, places to store food, gold, traps and monsters, and room for the adventurers to start lining up (as you build your dungeon you start to get some clues as to the kinds of characters you will meet). There is also a central board which shows the monsters and rooms available for that turn as well as the menu of actions you can take. The game is described as "worker-placement", but that isn't very accurate. There are 8 possible actions to take. Each action can be taken by up to 3 players, but the action will be slightly different depending on if you take it 1st, 2nd, or 3rd. Each player has a hand of 8 order cards corresponding to the 8 actions. 2 actions are unavailable each turn, so with the remaining 6 actions, players blind bid cards into 3 slots. After bidding, players reveal their 1st card and claim actions in player order, then they do the same with the 2nd and 3rd orders. This is where the meat of the game primarily lies. I typically don't care for blind bidding, but here it works well. There are lots of considerations in play. While there is definitely some uncertainty, it does not overwhelm the game. The only aspect of this that at all resembles "worker placement" is that if a 4th player seeks to perform an action, they are out of luck and lose the action. This only happened a couple of times in our game, but in a game with so few actions overall, it can be devastating.

The eight actions to build your dungeon are the heart of the game. Understand them and you are 90% there.

Adventurers come in standard types - Fighters always go in front, Thieves reduce damage from traps, Clerics heal, and Wizards can cast spells (randomly drawn). Three adventurers (plus possibly a powerful paladin if you are the evilest of all!) invade, conquering one room at a time. You assign a trap and monster each round. If there are any surviving party members, then they conquer the room and move on to the next. They stop when all are defeated or 4 rounds are up, which ever comes first. Conquered rooms are minus points at game's end and they lose all special powers. The scoring system rewards a balanced development strategy.

The game definitely has a ton of character and it is a pretty astounding design accomplishment. However, it is also very cumbersome to set up and even more cumbersome to teach. I don't mind these things usually, but a part of me wonders if this game is really worth all of the work. The game isn't long enough to build up a really impressive dungeon and most of the actions come in dribs and drabs. This was designed by Vlaada Chvatil, who also designed Galaxy Trucker, and there are definitely some similarities in the "build-it then watch it get destroyed" style of these games. In GT, it feels like you build more, adventure more, and generally have more fun in a shorter time frame. Dungeon Lords certainly has more strategy than GT, but it is also MUCH slower paced. I also think that losing an action by being the 4th player to choose it may be a bit too punishing. It's a good game though, pretty unique, and one I'd be happy to play again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Well, I like this better than Galaxy Trucker. Unfortunately, I find Dungeon Lords way more complex for the amount of fun I derived from it. There is a lot to keep track of. Fortunately, the iconography is most helpful.

The first half of the game is a little like Agricola in the sense that you are building up your engine. Worker/minion placement is determined by simultaneous reveal of actions. I found that interesting. Second spot is usually the most desirable.

The second half is the tear down phase, where heroes attack your dungeon. There is a puzzle element. You are trying to find the best combo of trap and monster to thwart the incoming party.

Like Agricola, I am mentally exhausted by the end. With Dungeon Lords, I am just happy to survive the ordeal and could care less about victory points. Yes, in my first game I scored less than zero and came in last. Mind you, I was playing against 3 other players with PhDs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

Dungeon Lords is a passable game that I can more or less dismiss in one sentence: way too much of way too little.

To wit: you will never earn much gold, collect much food, build many rooms, hire many imps, dig many tunnels, set many traps, recruit many monsters, fight off many adventurers, become all that evil, take many actions, or score many points. You will do all of these things, and it will take a while, but in the end it's a game with a large number of moving parts that creak once or twice before it's all over.

Everything rides on Vlaada Chvatil's trademark implementation of theme. The rulebook is laugh-out-loud hilarious, and that humor comes through in small doses during the game. It's certainly amusing to lure a Paladin into the countryside with your evil deeds, only to switch over to your best behavior so that the Paladin changes course and wrecks another player's dungeon instead of yours. But overall I can't see this game growing on me. It's quite procedural. The core mechanism of the game is simultaneous action selection crossed with turn-order resolution. So like Race for the Galaxy or Goldbrau, you have have to guess what other players are doing in order to maximize your own turn. I'm not a big fan of that in general.

After one play I feel like I've seen everything there is to see here. The differences that crop up from session to session may be enough for some players but for me I suspect they will be trivial. Dungeon Lords is a game of breadth, not depth.

Final note: there is no worker placement in this game. At all. Just as there is no deduction in Tobago. Someone get me a "mechanics police" microbadge, stat.

RATING: 9.996
Holy hell this game is difficult. I found it tougher than any game of Agricola. Good choices aren't apparent. It's easy to set yourself up for failure. I even had a turn where it effectively net me a loss. Lots of things to pay attention to. We were heeding the 2-player warning for "first time players", so we played a 3rd player, as a mock/dummy player. We just played random cards, and made our own rules for when and how to make choices.. The dummy player won by 5 points. He stayed low, got easy adventures, and cleaned them up pretty well.

Yet, none of it seemed unfair. I made bad choices and the game absolutely hammered me for it. It is an unforgiving game.

I heard the game was finicky, and I don't agree. While, as I said, there's a lot to pay attention to it isn't too complex or obfuscated by confusing iconography or obscure actions. 7 Wonders is more finicky than this.

The art is fantastic. The rule book is stellar (Though a little bit was difficult to reference.)

One of the finest games I've played. I had so much fun with this game. I can't wait to play it again.

RATING: 5
I was really pumped up about Dungeon Lords based on the theme and
the designer. Then I read the rules, and found out that it was a
game of blind action selection with only a tiny bit of information
about what others might do (the two cards they can't select), and
ridiculous amounts of fiddly chrome added on the side.

And the games I observed others play early on didn't encourage
me to try it either: the games took ridiculously long, some players
were completely out of the game very early on, and the reports were
that the battle system was nowhere near as interesting in practice
as the training puzzles suggested. I did eventually try it out a
couple of times, but it just felt very random and was far from
having enough meat on the bones for such a long game.

If there's one game whose apparent popularity I really boggle at,
it's DL.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My best game of 2009. It is easy to somehow relate this with Agricola, since the main part of the game involves work placement. Despite this, DL work placement selection occurs simultaneously, with players having some info about opponents options (each player has 2 unavailable orders every turn, and this is public knowledgment). These changes obviously imply two things: first, you need to try to guess what your opponents will do -taking into account their needs- and act accordingly. Second, it adds some randomness to the game (your efforts of guessing may be totally useless, and some unexpected move can end up punnishing you to great extents). For me, DL mechanics work better than Agricola ones, although this is, of course, a matter of taste.

The building phase also alows you to interact with the only really original mechanic of the game: the evilometre. This shows the evilness of every player, and can be altered via lots of different ways (hiring some monsters, spreading rumours about you...). It plays a very important role in the game, since it is used to select the adventurers that will attack each player and if someone will face also the frightenning Paladin.

Finally, the second phase of the game, when you "use" your monsters, traps and rooms to fight the adventurers trying to destroy you carefully built dungeon, is for me a great addition. This phase is not deep, since the meat of the game is the building phase, but it is really rewarding: In DL you build a dungeon, and then you use it and (hopefully) show everyone how well constructed it is. Compare this with Agricola, when you build a farm and then, well, the game ends.

All in all, DL is a very solid game that confirms Vlaada as my favourite game designer. Fun and original (the last one due to the evilometre), it may be not as outstanding as Space Alert but, well, since SA is my favourite game overall, I guess this is not a huge drawback. Highly recommendable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I think I want to love this game more than I actually do.  This is very much a worker placement game (except that you are using beautifully detailed plastic orcs...;-)  that is very visually appealing.  The game largely involves deciding what 3 actions you want to perform each turn (and in what order while figuring out which two actions will not be available for you the following turn as well).  Depending where your orc gets placed on the chart for each type of action, you will get more of something for less/more/maybe not receive something/etc.  The compensation levels for the different parts of the actions placement is very cleverly done.  After building up a dungeon and buying creatures and traps for four seasons, adventurers will troop through your dungeon seeking to explore and destroy.  Hopefully, you've prepared well for the adventurers and will prevent your dungeon from being destroyed too much!  Watch out for the paladin - if you go horribly evil, you best be prepared to handle a very dangerous foe!  Again, this was a good game - just not sure if I had fun - which is a bit of a problem!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Someone I was playing with said it was what Agricola was supposed to be, to which I say yes and no. Both are pretty Euro at heart, have kind of worker placement, are quite unforgiving and potentially have runaway leader problems, and give a puzzly aspecty of how do I make doing this worthwhile that is ultimately the draw of the game, at the expense of being really opaque your first play. Agricola has more of an engine building feel and most importantly has the variable setup with the cards. However, this one has a much more thematic scoring system. Yes I think the combat is overly convoluted (not surprising cause Chvatil), but defeating heroes for points feels a lot more cool than the negative point salad that is Agricola. Also I think experienced players could play this much faster, as the action programming allows for about half of the actions to be taken simultaneously. Finally, the way the actions are different strengths depending on who goes before/after seems to make the interaction feel greater than the sniping of the accumulating piles that is Agricola (regardless if it actually is more interaction or not), as you have to plan around it more.

However, at the end of the day this game still feels a bit too much like leaping through hurdles to score points, and the lack of variability really hurts it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This game seems to be inspired by the great PC game Dungeon Keeper.

It retains all the flavor and feel of said game while taking the best of worker placement and Vlaada's own mechanics, as well as tensions, from Galaxy Trucker and Space Alert.

There's certainly a heaping serving of luck and screwage, and much of the "just survive" element in this game; but perhaps that's much of the charm. Further once you have mastered the combat the game becomes an effortless fun-fest, and you can bask in the glory of your own evil.

It's more than what I expected, and on flavor alone this would get a 9; however the play is quite nice, and promises even better as it has a major set of unused components whose purpose is as yet unrevealed.

Can this designer fail? Not yet at least.

My review of Dungeon Lords:
1. The bits are good, except the quality of the paper tiles. They are easily damaged forthe first time to cut out. But the imps figures are impressive. The board design is fullly utilized.

3. The schedule of play is operational, less players' intrigue plans. The 1st briefing is long and got a lot of rules to memorize to get a whole picture of the game planning.
4. Got room to produce expansions afterwards: more monsters, more heroes....

RATING: 7.5
Although ostensibly a worker-placement game, Dungeon Lords has more in common with its predecessor, Galaxy Trucker, than it does with Agricola. This is a game about building something, and then putting it through the wringer and seeing if it breaks. This is a much more complicated game than Galaxy Trucker though, making it a true gamers game. There's more to keep track of (and manage carefully); three types of resources, and a whole range of different point-scoring options.

Despite the complexity, Dungeon Lords is fun. Though it's possible to make perfect plans in theory, there's just enough unpredictability that your combat strategy can be thrown off completely, and the oftentimes disastrous consequences can be hilarious. This really highlights the standout feature of this game: although it offers all the deep thinkiness of a traditional Euro, it's also got the fun typical of lighter fare. This will be problematic for those who expect that the best strategic thinker should win, but for those who follow Knizia's mantra (the goal is to win, but it's the goal that's important, not the winning), this game will provide a hard-to-find combination of fun and strategy that is quite satisfying.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Different theme for a worker placement, Dungeon Lords is a nice game with nice art and nice theme. It feels different than standard worker placements because of it, certainly like other Vlaada game.

RATING: 4
Wow, not since the crappyness that was "Tempus" have I been so disappointed in a game that should have been good.

DL is your standard shitty eurogame wrapped this time in a great little pasted on theme. Anyone who loved the Dungeon Keeper  video game series will instantly be drawn to the game, and the artwork and theme are applied about as well as one can expect from a euro.  

What makes this otherwise perfectly enjoyable game suck so back is the scoring. It is another game where most of the players (regardless of what you do) will score about the same. Then the winner will be decided by some rather random part of the game. (like top decking an unbalanced room tile for example). 

I for one hate playing something where at the end I am completely surprised by who wins, and that is exactly what happened in DL. 

The scores are so close by nature that really only a few thing make or break the winner... why play for all that time just to have one tiny part of the game crown a random champion... its like Goa all over again.. no thank you. 

It is a shame because it could have been a great little game.

RATING: 7
Latest - March 2010.  Playing without the standard rules' guessing game and using a regular worker placement scheme makes the game an 8 for me now.  I despise the guessing part, because that is what it is (after ~20 games that way that is my opinion).

Updated rating after many games (2/12/10) - standard rules only, no variants.  This game is okay, not special, even though I want it to be.

Did not meet my high expectations.  Make sure to read the rules TWICE before playing.  A minor rules miss-play could be fairly major.

I am starting to doubt the staying power of the game.  One bad season and you are out of contention for the win.  The fun factor of Galaxy Trucker is not here (did not expect it), but the satisfaction of finishing with positive points is a 'big deal' kind of thing.  Happens every game in our games (15 games and nobody has gotten zero or less points).  The disparity in scores can also be huge showing that just one player got what they needed and the others did not.

After many games, only the 4p version is interesting.  The game is the most unforgiving game I recollect playing.  Some good stuff here, but one little mistake and you can't recover from it.  This may end up sitting on the shelf not seeing much more action.  Frustrating, as we _really_ want to like it.

Added some new bits and house rules to push this to a 7. A game that just doesn't seem to want me to enjoy it.

EDIT 03/31/2013: Played again, this time with four people who had played before.  It definitely improves on replay a lot.  I partially played it this time to see if I wanted the Festival Seasons expansion.  Since that is now listed on my 'owned' list, I'd say it went well.  I played Lords of Waterdeep the following day and this just blew that experience away.  My go-to worker placement at least for now.

RATING: 9

And it always leaves us wanting more and wanting to play again and again.  This game has taken over our gaming group, and it looks to be a long, long time before we tire of it.

RATING: 3
Thief: Help me, priest guy, I'm hurt!
Priest: I'm sorry, that's a trap wound.
Thief: *Gurgle* It's a [i]spear[/i] trap!
Priest: Sorry, it's beyond my experience.
Thief: What the!? *Gurgle* I've been [i]stabbed[/i] by a [i]spear[/i], you festering twit!
Priest: I can't help you.
Thief: Gaaaa! (Stabs priest in eye with spear)
Priest: (Laughs) I can heal [i]that[/i]!

I don't know if I hate this game, but I definitely dislike. It's yet another version of the Eurovirus that tries to cajole its way into AmeriTrash cells by pretending to be about something cool.

But this is a puzzle game--and a very mechanical, uninteresting one at that--with a dungeon-building theme. The evil rating is fun, the rules are funny and well written and the thing looks great. But inside is a tedious and overlong math puzzle.

Honestly, now. The adventurers always walk in a certain order? The priest can heal injuries after a battle, but traps don't count as battle? Adventurers mostly die of fatigue, and you know exactly when that will happen? You have to kill the adventurers in a specific order to be "efficient"? Then add in a dungeon-building exercise with resource optimization, card management, bidding for resources, some resources being randomly and inexplicably unavailable to you at times?

This game will sucker in some ATers who think it looks like an exciting dungeon crawl experience. Not! This game will also repel some Eurogamers who think it looks like an exciting dungeon crawl experience. Not!

Sorry, it's just math. With pretty pictures. Look, I'd let Jessica Alba punch me in the face, just so I could honestly claim to have been touched by her. But I wouldn't let her do it a second time, and I wouldn't claim it had been fun. I feel much the same way about this game, except that it's not nearly as hot as Jessica Alba. Also, it cheated and punched me in the testicles.

RATING: 8.8
Seriously, this is better than [gameid=31260]. There are so many dimensions to contend with, and the scoring system, while still wonky, doesn't have that artificial balancing built in, so there truly are those 'different paths', although I was not on one of them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game intrigued me from the start.  I still wanted to try it out once before buying it, just to make sure.  After my first play I bought it immediately.  The idea of running the dungeon instead of pillaging it was an interesting theme.  This definitely feels like a worker placement, but the theme also makes it better.

In dungeon lords you have two years to try your hand at being the lord of your own dungeon.  One of the enjoyable parts of this game is reading the rules, they are corny sometimes, but they made me laugh several times.  During each year you get 4 rounds to build up your dungeon and then the adventurers that have been meeting up at your entrance attack.  They will either attack 4 times or until you capture them all, whichever comes first.  The second year only differs in that the monsters and adventurers are more powerful.

The actions you can do to improve your dungeon are getting food, mostly for hiring monsters; making yourself less evil (sometimes you get to see what spells the wizards are working on as well), which helps you not to attract the powerful paladin; digging for gold, used for a variety of tasks (including paying your taxes), digging more tunnels, giving you more place for rooms and options when the adventurers attack; getting more imps to work in your dungeon; buying traps; hiring monsters; and getting rooms.  At the end of the 2-4 rounds you will get an adventurer assigned you based on how evil you are.

After the year is over the 3 adventurers at your dungeon attack.  You get to choose where they attack, with some limitations, which monster and trap to send in and hopefully you can work it out so they don't conquer very many of your tunnels and rooms.

After the second year the scores are tallied, this is done based on number of tunnels, rooms, captured adventurers, etc.  I find that usually these scores leave people fairly close.  Then there are several titles in which the holder of the title will get 3 additional points, 2 each if multiple people are tied.  The titles are for things like most evil, most rooms, most tunnels, richest, most monsters, most imps, etc.

I really just love the idea of this game and it is a lot of fun.  There are some downsides, but if you have a regular gaming group these shouldn't be a big deal.  The rules take a while to explain.  It usually takes 20-30 minutes to give people a good feel for what is going on.  They included 4 setup attacks that you can go over as well so people understand how the attacking is going to work, if you go through these it will take another 15 minutes.  The first game is going to take at least 90 minutes.  However, after getting through these hurdles subsequent games shouldn't go a lot better.  There isn't any direct interaction; however, with the included expansion (you need to go to zman games to download the rules) you can add some interaction which makes things even more interesting.

I gave this a good rating because it is unique, at least in my collection, and I'd definitely never turn down a game.  However, I couldn't go too high, because it is just too difficult to bring out for a new group.  If you have a regular gaming group, I wouldn't pass this game up.

RATING: 7
Played once, with four players (none of whom had played before).

In a fun switch from the typical fantasy world, players are dungeon lords who are building an evil dungeon, which they must defend from pesky adventurers who want to destroy the player's creation.  The game is dripping with theme; players get to buy traps, hire monsters, and build rooms in their dungeon, all to thwart those pesky adventurers.

While game is fun, it took a long time to play (3+ hours) and there are a bunch of rules that come into play at various points that players kept forgetting (e.g., what the order of damage was in battle, exactly how thieves prevented damage, when you had to pay to use a trap).  

Players have to fight off two bands of heroes during the game.  In the first half of the game they're building up to fight the first round, and there are lots of fun options and tradeoffs (you can hire monsters, but it makes you more evil, and if you get too evil you attract a paladin).  However, it's difficult to predict what you'll have to pay for an item, and thus difficult to predict where you'll end up on important tracks (like the evil track, which determines which adventurer's you'll face and if you get the paladin attacking you). 

In summary, the theme is great, but there's a lot of randomness for a game of this length.

RATING: 6
I was expecting not to like this--after all, it is YET ANOTHER worker placement and resource conversion game--but somehow this didn't seem so redundant. Part of it is the clever theme, which provide moments of side entertainment of which something like, say, Keyflower is utterly devoid. But it's also that the simultaneous action selection sort of overrides the worker placement aspect. I'm not a huge fan of either mechanism, but at least it's variety.

That said, this felt more like a quirky pastime than a meaty game. Choices were fairly constrained, and there are an awful lot of hidden elements to the game, limiting your ability to lock in any long-term plans. Theme tends to wear off after a few plays for me, so I expect this to earn its six and then fall to the wayside.

RATING: 5
Colourful and fun looking art and theme hides a rules-heavy Eurogame maths-puzzle.

The only real player interaction is when other players fill up one of the options for the round before you get there, meaning you don't get to do anything at all (you are completely at the mercy of the first player marker in this department). Otherwise as with most Eurogames you could be sat playing on your own for all it matters. If you are a Eurogamer then good for you! You'll love sitting together in a group and ignoring each other like normal!

Unfortunately for me Dungeon Lords is rules heavy and requires lots of juice for little in the way of real choices or control. The final point being for two hours and that much effort I'd rather be playing Arkham Horror. ;- D

Rating may change after more plays...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A totally original combination of elements: a humorous theme (which I'm told is probably influenced by the old PC game Dungeon Keeper), a quirky worker placement/bidding war in the first half, and fiendishly difficult puzzles to solve in the 2nd half as you try to pick off the gang of adventurers before they run through your dungeon like a knife through butter. Vlaada has gone on record that his favourite contemporary designer is Stefan Feld, and I'm pretty sure that this game owes a debt to another famously punishing run-the-gauntlet kind of game, Feld's In the Year of the Dragon--it's got the same sense of seeing a catastrophe coming a long way off and juggling resources and actions in order to fend them off as best you can.

Further thoughts: this is an awfully long game for so much to hinge on random, unforeseeable elements (you can mitigate some of this by working hard to peek at the adventurers' upcoming cards, but other things are very random, like traps). I like the challenge of dealing with randomness but it can be frustrating to have hours of work scuppered by one bad combat card rather than by good play by your opponents.

Also: it's pretty harsh to get absolutely no compensation for being shut out of a spot. (As opposed to say Alchemists where at least you get a favour card for every two unused cubes.)

RATING: 9
In this 2-4 player game, each player is a dungeon keeper, attempting to get food, imps, traps and monsters. At the end of a building year, adventurers visit your dungeon and try to trash it. You use your monsters and traps to enslave the adventurers to earn points. It is an economic game with building, then using your building to collect points. This is Vlaada Chvatil's best game so far.

This is a very engaging game, with many tough decisions to make. The rules are funny to read, and it is a complex game. I really enjoy this game. Things are tense during the card plotting phase, where you wonder if you might get shut out, or if you will have enough money to do all the things you want. Then you might end up unexpectedly with what you did or did not want, and you have to adapt. I still haven't had the chance, nor the bravery to attempt to attract a paladin, but hope to change that sometime soon!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
My one and only playing of this was at BGG.Con '09, sometime around 2:00 A.M. so my views on the game may be a bit skewed.

I had heard so many positive things about this game and was really looking forward to trying it out. I love D&D and fantasy themes, so the fact that a strategy game, and not some lame dungeon crawler, used this as a background intrigued me.

What I found was a very fiddly rule system and a sort of blind-bidding mechanic that didn't thrill me. Yes, I get that you can figure out to a degree what other people might be doing, but that requires you to examine every player's board... something I really dislike doing in games. This game also relies on timing, and my sleep-deprived brain was nowhere near up to the task.

So, I give Dungeon Lords a tentative 6. I'm hoping that a second play with a clear mind will show me the greatness that others see. Stay tuned.

RATING: 2
Dungeon Lords' rulebook is so dense and uninituitive that just getting through it is a chore, game setup takes an eternity, and (as far as I can tell from the unintelligible instruction book) the "2-4 players" is a complete lie as, if you play with less than 4, the game requires you to play simplified versions of 1 or 2 players to bring the number up to 4.

Dr. Seuss was challenged to write a story using just 25 words and he wrote the wonderful Green Eggs & Ham. Vlaada should challenge himself to do something similar with components and rules and produce a game that is quicker to learn, faster to play, random enough that new players can win, yet deep enough to keep veterans coming back; In a word: Fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another great theme-laden game.  I really enjoy playing this game, however there are a few things that bring it down for me.  

It is a bit complicated for what it tries to accomplish, the rules are a bit daunting for new players...AND for me to explain as well.  This game has to be played with 4, the 3 and 2 player rules are annoying and never felt right to me.

You go through 4 phases of the game, 2 worker placement phases, and 2 battle phases.  In the worker placement phase you are placing workers to hire monsters, build your dungeon, and collect various rewards that will help you during the game.  In the battle phases heroes come to your dungeon and you have to kill them.

Although the game is a bit complicated, and can really only be enjoyed by exactly 4 people, it really is a fun and engaging game.

RATING: 8
There are plenty of similarities to the designers other game, Galaxy Trucker. In both there are several rounds (2 in DL, 3 in GT) where you build up your resources and then there is resolving phase at the end of the round where you battle "against the game". In Dungeon lords you have better chances to influence what you'll get at this final phase as in GT it is much more random. Overall DL is more of a gamer's game out of these two but still retaining plenty of humour that was what made GT a great game. 

Rulebook is using this humour well to make you remember things that otherwise would easily be forgotten but there is still (as in GT) a bit too much tiny rules that you easily forget. The game is quite rules-heavy but a very good game.

Pretty much a four player only game.

RATING: 5
Firstly the art work, production and theme are all excellent. A great start.

The game is about building a dungeon and placing measures to ensure it survives. Damage limitation is the name of the game here. The bulk of the building and planning is a parallel worker selection game mechanism, which is nice enough and the semi-blind selection adds a bit of variation from the swath of worker placement games that have come before. The first part is in preparation for the “fun” part of the game where you see how well your dungeon can withstand assault by do-gooder heroes using resources you built/collected in the first part.

Looking at a solid game then? Well, almost. The game is really let down by the scoring. Virtually everything either gives you points or takes points away so scores are pretty close by the end of the game as long as you didn’t mess up too badly. The winner is then determined by some arbitrary scoring device or the outcome of a few lucky events. Players will have no real concept of who will win until the final tally. With so much investment and micromanagement this is really frustrating and the game isn’t fun or frivolous enough (despite appearances, this is  firmly in the euro game camp) to be an experience game where no-one cares about the outcome.

If you can live without all the lovely pieces and the desire to be a dungeon lord, then playing In the Year of the Dragon will give you a similar experience, but where your strategy actually matters.

RATING: 7
This is good! The overall idea is simple and satisfying: compete for resources to build a "mechanism", then test it; score or lose points based on the outcome. The theme is very well executed. The rules are clear and consistent: they are also easy to learn, given the variety of options available. The components are well produced and helpful, particularly the player aids and the board.

My only caveat is that [i]Dungeon Lords[/i] seemed a tiny bit light and too short. I think it needs a third round ("year"). This might be unfair - I have been playing [i]Le Havre[/i] and [i]Shipyard[/i], which are also "build a mechanism" games and quite a bit longer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Most important: It's overall a multiplayer solitaire, and I'm getting increasingly annoyed at this, let's say "New School of contemporary board game design". 

Second most important: It's a thematic puzzle, pure mental arithmetics. It's like: I have 3 food now. If I play that action card on my last free spot, I might be able purchase the troll, which would make sense if the paladine came to my dungeon after the next season. But I in order to inflict 4 damage, I need another food, which I may only get if I play the food action on the last spot blablabla.... and so on. It will give you a headache. And in addition to that, the rules are twisted and complex. Be sure you won't recall all rules during your first plays.

Sounds like your game? Heavily focussed on skill, no randomness, no kingmaking, no leader bashing? All true, except for the randomness. In short: I have the impression that skill alone won't make you win this game. I have not figured out yet how to really play it well - but it seems to me that attemps to analyse and calculate the optimal move are just futile, because you can't be sure what your opponents are going to do, and that will decide if your plans work out or not. Now, you think this could be mitigated by judging the needs of your opponents? In theory - yes; in practice - hardly. There is so much calculation involved for your own moves alone that I don't think anyone can calulate his opponents' moves at the same time.

Yes, it's full of humour, yes, it's got a hilariuos theme, yes it's fine, it's well designed, the rules even have a plausible background, and reading the rules is an experience of its own. The game, however, doesn't create much atmosphere. Combat isn't exciting, it's determined under the assumption of perfact play. The only question is: Did you play perfectly? 

All in all, the game represents the all the aspects of current gaming I dislike the most. The theme alone makes it worth playing once in a while. However, I've had several invitations to play DL within the previous months, but rejected them all in the favour of other games. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Just had two plays, so this rating may change. First play (3p) was rough, missed a few important rules. Second play was much smoother and *felt* faster, even though it still took a solid two hours. I think we were just feeling more fluent with the game.

Terrific cute theme hiding a hardass, take-no-prisoners, gamers game. The basic shape of play is pretty easy: compete for three positions amongst eight different actions, assign workers to do things in your dungeon, then deal with a team of three or four adventurers coming in to wreck your shit. It's similar in the arc to Galaxy Truckers, only not frenetic and ... well, the demolition derby inside the dungeon is just not as much fun as with your GT spaceship.

Actual play is astonishingly deep and varied, and I suspect it'll take dozens of plays to really figure out how to play it well. Those eight actions are very hard to choose from, the game just doesn't give you nearly enough turns to get everything done, and the actual beat-the-heroes stage is a wicked-hard logic puzzle.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great theme, great bits. You know you're going to enjoy the game when it comes with an official Evilometer. Game play is preparation for combat, then combat, then repeat. Preparation consists of competing for worker placement spots (for gold, food, monsters, traps, digging more tunnels, etc). Your placements are planned in secret then executed 1 by 1 in turn order, so you're never sure what you may need to pay as each spot has a different type of payment depending on the order you go in - some it's better to be first, others it's better to be middle or later. Sometimes payment is up or down on the evilometer which is important because the more evil you are, the tougher the adventurer you're going to have to fight off in the combat phase. Combat is just inflicting as much hit points as you can as quickly as you can on the adventurers allocated to you using the traps and monsters that you've bought - no dice. It's a nice little puzzle on how best to play your monsters and traps to beat them as fast as you can. Each turn one stays alive, a part of your dungeon is lost - so build plenty. Scoring is varied - points for kills, most monsters, most tunnel, etc. There's a good balance of interactive luck, some luck with magic cards that come out in combat, but room for plenty of decision making. I'm looking forward to playing more.

RATING: 8
Il Gamer's Game per eccellenza: difficoltà sopra la media, tema molto nerd e grafica che non accetta mezze misure, piace o fa schifo. Spassosissime le due fasi di gioco "pianificazione impietosa" (nella quale si pagano cari cincischiamenti nell'ordine di piazzamento) e quella di azione. 
Ma quello che fa godere davvero sono i particolari, nel tema e nelle meccaniche: da una parte le tasse da pagare al Ministero dei Dungeon, le caratteristiche dei mostri, il "razzismo" verso i fantasmi; dall'altra finezze come il metodo che assegna eroi più forti ai Lord più cattivi (per non parlare del paladino che scende in campo se stuzzicato), il sistema per impedire di giocare due volte la stessa carta, la trasposizione dei poteri dei vari personaggi, e così via. 
Tema molto americano e meccaniche molto tedesche: che sia il Sacro Graal dei Giochi da Tavolo? Un gioco non per tutti, ma sicuramente un piccolo capolavoro.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game has a great theme, and the gameplay is pretty good, but quite hard to explain to new players.  There is some simultaneous action selection (queued like RoboRally), so if you have a strong distaste for that you may not enjoy it, but I didn't find it to be that offensive.  Basically, each player secretly selects 3 actions every turn; usually the first player to select any given action gets the least benefit at a small cost, the second player gets the best deal, the third player pays a lot for a good benefit, and the fourth player gets hosed (though it's rare for all 4 players to select the same action in a given turn).  These actions allow you to gather resources to build up your dungeon and fight off a series of adventurers.  Most of the points at the end of the game revolve around how well you were able to stave off those adventurers, and how impressive your dungeon is overall.  Where I find the game lacks is in actually fighting the adventurers: this is a rules-heavy process that isn't well-connected with the rest of the game.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 8
Based on the PC game (Dungeon Keeper), Dungeon Lords puts the players in the role of a dungeon manager of sorts. 

Build tunnels and rooms, recruit monsters to defend the dungeon, collect taxes and food, recruit personnel to work and at the end of the year defeat the adventurers that arrive to destroy your work.

A lot of stuff to manage, a very well implemented theme and great components, make this one a real winner.

Another really impressive game from Vlaada Chvatil!

RATING: 8.5
Great theme. Gameplay feels like some kind of combination of Kingsburg, Puerto Rico & Galaxy Trucker (or other similar games along those lines).

Luck is enough of an element so as to keep things unpredictable, and varied between games.

Components are great except for some of the cardboard pieces not being cut very well - tricky to punch without tearing them.

Rules are very well done, though a bit long for the complexity of the game.

Also takes a while to teach people how to play - a decent grasp of the combat basics are needed before you can even begin to touch on the main rounds of play. A first learning game with new players takes too long - the first "year" is more than enough, with scoring taking place and a quick summary of what would be different in the 2nd year for another time.

Plays well with only 2 players, even though you each need to handle cards for a "dummy" player - something I normally avoid but is easily managed here, and actually becomes part of your tactical play.

RATING: 6
In this game, you are an evil overlord, trying to build a nice dungeon for yourself, but your general evilness keeps attracting pesky hero tiles that will try to destroy what you've built. This game uses a fantastic action prediction system where you need to guess what action cards your opponents have selected to be able to resolve your actions fully. The overall goal of the game is building the best dungeon while trying to take the least amount of damage from the heroes. This is also an interesting balancing act, as killing the heroes is worth a lot of points, but you are always risking biting off more than you can chew. 

The reason I didn't keep this is the board and charts, while pretty, detract substantially from clarity, and with fewer than 4 players this game absolutely does not work. The game introduces dummy players which eliminates the whole point of predicting your opponents. It just wasn't compelling enough to keep for it being so restrictive.

:nostar: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:nostar: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 6
I slightly surprised myself by enjoying Dungeon Lords as it includes several features that I am not usually fond of: worker placement, blind bidding and a fantasy theme. And as I sat through the lengthy rules explanation, I feared the worst. But it is really not as complex to play as it is to explain and the excellent sense of humour helps to carry everything off. It's essentially two games: a worker placement/resource management phase to equip your dungeon with rooms, traps and monsters; followed by a combat phase in which your dungeon is visited by heroes and you must puzzle out the best way to capture them before they destroy all your hard work. The blind-bidding mechanic for placing the workers is really clever, though can lead to AP as there is a lot of information to take into account. Once the blind orders have been made, resolving them is very quick and easy. The combat phase is reminiscent of Galaxy Trucker as you see whether your design is going to hold together or be wrecked. There's no player interaction in this part of the game, but it's still good fun seeing how everyone fares. In my first game I found it hard to even really think about the final scoring as I was too busy learning the ropes, but I'd be interested in trying again. Not a game I'll be buying, but a lot better than I had feared.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It more or less works, though it can make things more random. Also something has to be done to control the evil-o-meter for this to really work out well.

RATING: 8
What a brutal and awesome game. I can't remember the last time a game has thrashed me this hard and left me asking for more.

Dungeon Lords blends a handful of my favorite mechanics into one intense experience. Perhaps my favorite part of the game, and the most unforgiving, is when the heroes invade. Only the most careful planning will prevent your dungeon from being totally demolished.

Overall: It doesn't matter how few points I score in Dungeon Lords. This is simply a hilariously fun ride. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
By the designer of Through the Ages, so I had high hopes for this one, and I have not been disappointed. In a sense, it's an economic engine game, with a pause at halftime and another at the end to see how well your engine withstands non-player attacks. There are lots of choices and plenty to think about. It will take practice and familiarity to play it well.

The theme is silly but stronger than you often get in economic games. (Don't let the cartoony art fool you into thinking that this is a family game.) Components are nice overall, except that we had trouble punching some of the cardboard in our copy and had to glue up some tears.

If you have trouble with busy artwork on boards, you might want to avoid this one. The board art carries a lot of useful info and I think it's laid out well, but the overall impression can be one of confusion and "too much detail" until you get used to it. The rules are a bit long and complex and the bits a bit fiddly: I don't mind this but you might.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Cleverly put together, combining a puzzle with some blind worker placement and a resource engine game. Each part is simple enough so that the whole game is approachable. The player interaction is subtle, but I think it is what determines the outcome of the game. The obvious parts are keeping an eye on the leader's likely titles and trying to tie them, and planning the timing of your card plays. The less obvious and I think key part is manipulating your place on the evilometer: a party of one or two character types that you have planned for is a pushover, other combinations - especially ones you didn't plan for (lots of thieves when you have mainly traps) can be a disaster. Good fun.

RATING: 8
I think this has the potential to be one of the best games I own - I love the mechanics, both the risk/reward and mind-reading of the action-selection phase and the puzzly nature of the combat, and also like how well the theme is integrated into the game. (I must mention the rulebook in this regard - easily the funniest I have ever read, eclipsing even that of Dungeon Petz, yet without getting annoying. It explains the game pretty well too.)

But I've only ever got to play two-and-a-half games of this, despite owning the game since mid-2012 - all with the "first game" rules. For some reason, my wife and most others in my regular game group didn't like the game - the main complaint seeming to be the arithmetical nature of the combat phase. So I'm very rarely going to get to play this, and have sort of given up trying, especially as there are plenty more games which I love and do get to play.

RATING: 8.4
(+) Great fun combining a wicked worker placement (with a serious twist by using the order cards) with a sometimes mind bending puzzle figuring out ways to manage the evilometer to attract the right heros and trying to efficently defeat them during the battles
(+) Magnificent theme: this is a heavy euro-game which really does emerge you in its fantasy-theme
(+) Clever and original gaming mechanics, the game truly feels different
(+) The game is very challenging, but because of this, it is very rewarding to players who manage to pull things off
(+) Very nice artwork

(-) Some components are great quality (wooden pieces, imps, cardboard tunnels, adventurers, monsters, rooms, ...), but the central board and player boards are printed on thin cardboard which doesn't remain flat on the table: such a shame for an otherwise beautifully realized game!
(-) Explaining the game is a time-consuming affair. It takes quite some effort for the one explaining and the new players learning the game
(-) I would have liked the worker placement phase to have lasted a little longer: building up your dungeon is great fun, but there aren't a lot of actions or imps to spend on tunneling, building rooms, buying traps and monsters, and getting a chance to actually get full use of your rooms (as you also need your precious actions to make sure you get extra imps, maintain your monsters and collect gold and manage the evilometer). The expansion 'Festival Season' solves this problem very elegantly.

RATING: 7
Typical Czech Games games very much in the style of Galaxy Trucker or Space Alert. RATING: 6
11-03-2014. Rated after one play. Hmm, Vlaada recycles many of the game mechanics from other games, I started noticing after playing Dungeon Lords, after Petz and MK.

In this game you play a dungeon lord, and in 8 rounds you attempt to meet a goal (kill a bunch of adventurers dastardly trying to retire you), while keeping most of your dungeon intact.

The game is FUN! It's a weird kind of fun, since it's a heavy, thinky game. But figuring out what to do in what round is nice. Artwork is ok. Iconography acceptable. This game IMHO would really benefit from a big graphic overhaul and dungeon petz like components..

This game is cool.. and I think a lot of people really can appreciate it for what it does. I see the appeal of the game and I like it.. but just don't want to play it all to often. So, all in all, I think the game is good, but mostly not for me, a 7 rating reflects that best; a 6 is too low for me.

RATING: 6
A game that is loaded with rules, intricacies, and strategy, so it should be a real chore to play, right? Well, you'd be wrong. The theme is so fun and so present, that the game never feels like work. It just feels like you are a normal dungeon executive, trying to make an honest living in spite of all those annoying adventurers. This is a special game, one of the best heavy Euro-games ever designed. It has tons of personality, and in spite of it's intricate rules, it flows very well. Highly recommended.

*edit* I eventually traded the game away. I was still enjoying it, but I think that for all the rules and complexity the game contains, you actually don't have a lot of say in what happens to you. I tend to really like "experience games" but this is one where the complexity may be too high for what it is. Still, it's good for about five plays.

RATING: 9
Never was a strategy game so punishing and laid so much waste to your plans as this. Heavy, requires very careful planning and programming of orders requires predicting what other players are likely to do. If you're very lucky and everything goes according to plans, your result goes off the chart. However, something always goes wrong. The randomness is very limited, but you never can fully predict what will happen anyway and can't plan on doing something for sure, you always must have a backup plan. It punishes heavily for every mistake, and sometimes even if you didn't make any. Makes for a very stressful game and definitely not for everyone - you'll either hate it or love it. I love it.

RATING: 5

There are penalties throughout the game that influence when and what players will purchase for their dungeons, with the Galaxy Trucker trial at the end to see if your preparations were up to scratch. Wrap it up in a fun dungeon theme with monsters and imps, add a solid card mechanic, present it with nice bits and you have a good game. It's quite long but doesn't feel like it. A good game just for the fun of it that has you wanting to bring it out just one more time because surely you can do better next time.

RATING: 8
When I first read the rules and my wife and I did our first play-through, I thought, "This game hates me. I work to build up stuff, but then it just gets destroyed."

But then I read the rules again (I hadn't missed much, thankfully), and then we played it again. It's wonderful. I no longer think the game hates me. It asks a lot from the players. The rules are fun to read and well thought out, and the graphic design is intuitive and fantastical. But the mechanics don't baby you and coddle you along. You better be ready to pay your taxes. And if you are recruiting a monster, you better be ready for the pay day when it comes up. And did you take on too much evil during the building phase? Well, you better be ready to back it up because you're gonna get attacked a lot.

So a 2nd play was definitely good to help clarify stuff, but also get a better feel for how everything works together. It's a great game that I look forward to playing more.

RATING: 6
Mechanism-wise, I really liked the cardplay, and the fact that you lose the two later cards, but those later cards will often garner you better things.

However...

I felt that the cardplay is the only meaningful and interesting decision I make in the game.

Battles are just mathy puzzles where play seems fairly obvious.

I know this was a first game for two of us, but it still took too long--it's especially annoying that we played for so long but there were only two "battles" in all that time.

And when you do have combat, there are 47 steps to go through. "Wait, the priest can heal, but only damage from attacks that happen this turn? And traps don't count as attacks? Wha?" And the spells are awkward. "Ohhh...what's the next spell?! Wait. It only affects Player C since he's the only one with a wizard; why do I care again? Oh, fatigue, I guess. But fatigue only affects the person in the front of the party? Wha?"

It tries to be both a dungeon crawler and a euro, and it doesn't really succeed at either.

RATING: 9
While competition was fierce from the beginning, a sign of a great game is the ability to have a ton of fun while loosing big-time (see Through the Ages).  Even with a light dose of (mis)luck and with what I foresee as even more 'screwage' issues than I at first imagined, these elements add even more color to one of the strongest themed games I've ever played.  Not much else is as fun as building your customized dungeon (flexible enough to dictate a focused strategy) by way of analyzing other lords' and predicting their dastardly moves.  Just as forward-thinking and planning-oriented as other phase-controllers/role-order-predictors like Puerto Rico and Race for the Galaxy but with an uber-fun WP-puzzle.  I found it to be as interactive as I expected and that wasn't a lot but the room for variants, expansions and other cool goodies is immense.

RATING: 7
Was expecting and hoping for a fantasy themed Galaxy Trucker with the way cool evil dungeon building theme.

Was a little disappointed that wasn't what was hiding inside the box and what was there appeared to be a kinda convoluted beast which seemed intricate and fiddly and hard to follow and understand, albeit with very cool components and really fun artwork.

The first game is definitely very much a learning game, but we had a good rules 'splanation and it started to make sense by the halfway point.

The second game flowed a lot smoother (if you've only had the one crack at it do yourself a favour and revisit it) and all the bits made sense and fit together very nicely.  I can understand why it would be tricky to explain or wrap your head around, but really it does make sense after a bit.  I feel a little gyped that it isn't the zany, light fare that I expected (largely due to the fun and zany theme).  But that's my own fault because what's here is a very good and well designed efficiency/worker placement game with a novel and great theme which has been very well integrated.  But behind the theme there still is a good engine that stands on it's own merits.

There's a fair amount of thinking involved and it'll take you about 2 hours to play.  It's not Galaxy Trucker, but it was never meant to be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Dungeon Lords has led me to conclude that Vlaada Chvatil and I do not mix (I have also played Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization and Galaxy Trucker). I can appreciate the design and the many original ideas, but they get diluted in a mountain of fiddly procedural rules. Fiddly procedural rules are okay with me if the theme is strong, but the theme is not strong here. Charming and quirky, yes, but it's farming in fantasy clothing (and farming in Agricola isn't even a strong theme to begin with). The decision mechanisms are better and more interesting than that in Agricola, but the painful Adventurer phase is just a pile of procedural crap that's designed to confuse newbies and make them lose (since you basically have to learn a new sub-game only after you realized you didn't prepare adequately through not knowing the nuances of it). Just let me feed two food per head and be done with it.

In keeping with the dungeon-keeper theme, I had a lot more fun, and got into it a lot more, when I unleashed little demons in Claustrophobia. The whole experience was a clunky collection of mechanisms, without theme to glue them together.

This, like other Chvatil games, and a lot of Uwe Rosenberg games, is all about not committing a mistake while you grapple with the rules. Then after you get comfortable and can play mistake-free, you learn nobody else can play like you, or people have already moved on to the next game.

At least this game does not derive replayability from hidden information, unlike Agricola's shameful cards.

4+1

RATING: 7
This new take on the old Dungeon Keeper PC game is a pretty good game.  Teaching and setup are pretty long - especially the teaching.  The game itself is about two hours with people who generally don't get locked up, but teaching and setup tacks on nearly an additional hour.  Thematically, it works, though an element like the tax man seem a little tacked on.  I like how the tougher heroes go to the most evil DL.  It's a solid, good game, but the long play/teaching time takes some of the punch out of the fun.

I had meant to downgrade this for a while, but had forgotten.  I'm starting to get the feeling that Vlaada's games are not for me.

So, four years after playing this for the first time, I played it again and I have to say that I really appreciated it more the second time around.  I'm not going to say it's my favorite, but it was definitely better than I remember.

RATING: 5
Nice enough parallel play engine game, but really I've just about reached my limit on these kinds of games.  I like what they did with it, but the theme is only in the text and art.  Honestly, this could have just as easily have been about a barbarian horde in medieval europe or weeds in your garden.  Yes, it is cool that it isn't, but that only goes so far when you expect something so daring.

It is an elaborate multiplayer solitaire game again. Where interaction is reduced even further than the traditional Princes of Florence model by creating blind programing of actions (effectively blind worker placement).  While intuiting things is fun and all, it insures that there just isn't much reason for other players to be there.

I've got to say with all of these solitaire worker placement/drafting efficiency games coming out I'm really starting to miss the days of bidding for everything.

The longer I stay away from this the less interested I am in going back.  It just seemed like too much work for a long dry mathy puzzle.  It seems especially strange to compare the results of people's actions to one another to determine a winner when so much of what happens to you in particular is random and completely inconsequential of what other players are doing.

Oh yeah, the imps are cool.

RATING: 7.5
1 partial game

The rules are pretty straightforward, but it took a LONG time to actually get through them! But basically, the game goes like this:
- you go through 2 years, each with 4 seasons.
- Each season, you send your workers out using 3 cards, 2 of which will be unavailable to you next season. The cards are resolved one by one, in turn order -- you place your minion in the matching action area; but in a 4er game, only 3 spaces are available.
- After all workers are placed, each is resolved -- usually in order of placement but occasionally reverse. Spaces give you various things (e.g., food, money, tunnels, rooms, monsters), usually at different costs depending on order of arrival to action space. You are building a dungeon on your player board, and acquiring traps and monsters to defend it.
- At the end of 3 of the seasons, you deal with an event (e.g., pay for monsters again) and also allocate the randomly drawn adventurers -- the weaker ones go to the nicer players (lower on the Evilometer). 
- At the end of each year, the adventurers go through your dungeon: use your traps and monsters to try to fend them off ("capture" them) through up to 4 rounds of combat. Each round they survive, they conquer part of your dungeon.
- After the 2nd year, you get VP for a variety of things (e.g., size/composition of dungeon, monsters, position on Evilometer); most VP wins!

A cool, different take on worker placement! Very puzzle-y: figure out the best way to use your traps/monsters. But also get into everyone else's head a bit: which action are they going to choose, and when? You want to optimize both your action choice and order or placement (programmed orders) to try to get the best version of the action you want. And you would like to jockey for position on the Evilometer to get the adventurer you can handle, too. I suspect all this is beyond me, but I do look forward to trying the full game some time!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Fun, but I'm not sure how it will hold up to repeated plays.
