=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is an excellent, almost sort of tower-defense game.  If you have ever played the small card game Boss Monster, imagine that but with a lot more depth and meat on the bones.

In this game each player is the lord of their own dungeon.  Using worker placement mechanics each player will spend each round gathering resources and using these resources to stock their dungeon with traps, monsters, and special rooms to combat against adventurers who will traipse through dungeons in an attempt to conquer our homes.

The action selection is done really well.  Each player has a hand of 10 cards however two of those are unusable each round and often being the first one to get to an action space is the worst position to be in but being too late and there might not be space for you at that action at all.  There is a lot of deduction and mind games to try and work it out so you get the best spot at the different action spaces.

I also really like the "evilometer" mechanic.  The strongest heroes will always go after the most evil dungeon lord.  You gain evil through various actions and hiring certain monsters but you can also reduce this amount.  The game is completely full of flavor and theme in fact and I love that the rulebook describes thematically all of the game effects and they all actually make sense.  Combining rich theme with complex original mechanics has always been something Chvatil does better than pretty much anybody else I think.

Fair warning, this game is mean.  It is equal-opportunity mean though and the game is more about managing and mitigating the amount of smacking around you take rather than eliminating it altogether because I'm not sure it is possible.  In my first play I couldn't pay my taxes (that's right, you have pay taxes twice during this game) and ended up taking a 12 point hit due to it.  I had 9 points at the end of the game and the winner had 20.  That's right, if I could have paid my taxes I would have won by 1 point.  Talk about getting beat up...and I'm still excited to play again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
I'm not much into fantastic themed games. I'm more like a serious player who occasionally can play one of these kinds of game. Dungeon Lords it's not my number one choice for anything: I find the boards and ll the gaming materials too noisy. I think strategy is very much weaker than luck and I guess the time of play and, above all, the interactivity are both poor. Disclaimer: time of play could be under the 2 hours which can happen with experienced players.

The way players can choose their actions is interesting. Nothing new here but I like this particular mechanic. Knowing better the people we are playing with is a good advantage because Dungeon Lords has this guessing characteristic. The position to place your workers is very important.

I didn't find the "being a bad guy" thing a good advantage. In fact I think players who make a conservative play are better rewarded than the ones who don't. And I'm not sure if I like that in the game. I tend to enjoy better, games that reward aggressive and innovative strategies, although I admit I'm not usually one of those players.

I will play this game from time to time but I won't be playing it very often. I found it fun enough to deserve the table occasionally.

RATING: 7
A Chvátil game through and through, this game will leave you with the highest highs and the lowest lows.  No one in my group ends the game with an average score.  Either a player does well or gets crushed.  This might be what brings our group back for sessions so often.  No one ever feels like they have this game mastered.

This game introduces a great inference spin on worker placement that is most enjoyable with 4 players.  The evil track accompanied by the paladin hero, this game's risk/reward mechanism, adds greatly to the theme.  The random event card also adds the right amount of tension to the game's rounds.  As with other Chvátil games, resource management in Dungeon Lords presents a great challenge.

I love the expansion rules added to the original game for free.  I only subtract two-points from this game's rating because the "title" bonus scoring room skews the final point total and outcome of the game to greatly.

RATING: 6
Much of the charm of dice in RPGs comes down to the surprise. You're in it for a good time and a good story, so what you're going to remember are the natural 20s that get spun into bizarre, unlikely successes ("and that's how the halfling killed the dragon with a banana"), or the 1s that turn a sure thing into a pratfall. The worst thing that can happen is easy, dull progression.

Dungeon Lords is euro-strategy worker placement that uses hidden information to much the same effect. Players build up their dungeons (tunnels, rooms, guardian monsters, traps) to try and defeat the heroes who come adventurin' twice during the game. But actions are chosen by pre-programming (players simultaneously choose where their three workers will go) and first in to a given location isn't necessarily best dressed. There is a very good chance of not getting what you want.

Payoffs are uneven, and position on the evilometer is crucial: which heroes players attract depends on their level of evil (and the evilest run the risk of attracting the attention of a paladin), but different heroes also require different rooms, traps and monsters to defeat. Small differences can go a long way.

It's a clever design, with wonderful theme, but I'm not convinced that the whole is better than the sum of its parts. It's a genre I just don't understand,  part agonized second-guessing and part crapshoot, but not really in a push-your-luck kind of way; it's too much a strategy game for that. I suppose the target audience is the fan of Welcome to the Dungeon who thinks it would be improved by 90+ minutes of worker placement. 

I don't want a lot of "fun" surprises in my strategy games, but that's basically what the game is about: the game tries its best to hurt you and at one point or another it manages it. There is a lot of hidden information and a lot of points swinging on minor differences -- and it doesn't take much in a game where a winning score can easily be less than 30 points.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[Z-man games 2010 edition] {Dungeons; programming, tile-laying; optimal 4p}  Dungeon Lords is a thematic game.  Even though it comes with attractive game components, it is only suitable for experience players because of the difficult decisions and tight resources.  Essentially it is a resource management game - players have to use their resources (Imp, food, gold, evil) carefully to build a dungeon and score.

It is a multi-faceted game.  However, this is only a tactical game due to the random draw of monster tiles, room tiles, adventurers, event cards.   It is quite hard to fix a strategy in first 2 rounds and stick to it.  Players have to adjust their tactics as the game runs.  Limited mixture of monsters and rooms is one problem - which makes the game less dynamic and luck (the timing when a monster appear can be quite significant).

It is a good 2p / 3p game - esp. players have more controls in this situation.  However, with 4 experienced gamers, Dungeon Lords is very fun and very brain-burning.

It is an interactive game.  There are many competitions on resources and actions.  And, forcing another player to take a strong adventurer is fun.  It is not easy to teach Dungeon Lords as there are many fiddly rules and the complexity of game flow.  Players may need to try 1 or 2 turns to experience the game.

A typical game runs around 1.5 hour.  Very good.  I would recommend this game to most players.

The game components are top notch.  The tiles may wear fast upon multiple plays.  The illustration is attractive and informative.  The Imps are cute.  The boards are nicely design and comes with good player aids.

RATING: N/A
I heard the opinions: "Pure chaos". I understand them - the game is not intuitive and you need more than a single play to catch its nuances. But, I don't share these opinions at all! If you devote some time to go into DL details, the game starts to shine. While it's the euro-game, the theme is built in very well. And the battles with adventurers... - very tasty. 
Many decisions needed: to be more evil and attract the paladin or to take easy guys having better reputation? How to plan your actions to have a good profit and not to be stuck in the next rounds? But it's not obvious - you should observe others, check the order of the events (pay day, taxes), rooms available. There are also many details that you should remember about - if you forget, the "impression of chaos" can be strong...

In addition: incredible graphics, fantastic rulebook. 

Number of plays: 7.

RATING: 6.5
I found the really first play through the first half of the game tedious, boring and sort of confusing, but after the first attack I found it extremely enjoyable.  It is certainly a daunting game for new players and has a learning curve/slow pace when first learning with people that detracts from their enjoyment.

Granted this is a review of one play, but as many have said, I loved dungeon keeper so I really got into the theme. Mechanically its plenty solid, there's alot going on and a fair amount of balancing and thinking required as the game relies on the possibility of you choosing the wrong actions, or sequence at a given time.  Because there's so much going on its a VERY fiddly game, but once you get the hang of it the play is pretty fast.  I think it has potential to be good, but it requires multiple plays to reach that point.  There's lots of fun options that can come up. I like the little details on the hero's as they gain levels and the board itself.  I will say this though, the minion figure is a T-rex head, I don't care what anyone says.  Box art could be better, but that's pretty minor when it comes to a pretty frustrating but enjoyable game.  The box interior is total fail however, especially with so many cards/chits/markers so it loses points there, nothing worse than getting a giant hole to stick your stuff and having to reorganize it every time you want to play.

It loses half a point for having really crappy chits that sometimes rip even when your carefully punching them out.

RATING: 7.5
[i][size=18]Defending your lair against Adventurers since 2009[/size][/i]

I had actually played this once as a 2 player game about 7 years ago and didn't enjoy my play a whole lot.  But playing it again as a 4 player game, I really enjoyed it.  Dungeon Keeper was one of my favorite computer games growing up, so I love the theme of building a dungeon and fighting against heroes.  The mechanics are also pretty interesting with the way that you have to guess what other people are going to do.

I've played many games of [thing=97207][/thing], which I love, and I always thought it was a much better game than Lords.  But, now I'm not convinced.  Petz also has a very amusing theme and interesting mechanics, but it can also be pretty swingy with regard to which cards you draw.  Lords felt a lot less random.

Either way, it's a solid game and reaffirms Vlaada as my favorite designer.  I'd love to play this again soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After my first two plays, maybe I am in an odd state of infatuation.  Hard to say.  I really like the game.  I have read all kinds of statements, opinions, ideas, whatever about Dungeon Lords.  So I'll try to put down my own ideas, without much subjectivity.

First off, its hard to not be drawn to the game if slick presentation is your thing.  The boards, quite busy and overloaded, are attractive.  The game, all laid out, just looks cool, if not a bit hyperactive.  

Second, it is a ton of stuff to understand on your first play.  There is a pile going on, almost like two games in one, with worker placement first, then a hard to understand battle sequence following.  It all is pressed together in quite a frenetic package that is a bit overwhelming upon initial approach.

And it can be killer hard.  Not dense, but more tough to go anywhere.  The game really can beat you up.  But at least I have found that I still really enjoy playing.  

So my early analysis is that I like Dungeon Lords.  It is a great attempt at bringing a more interesting theme together with the worker placement genre (I am on record saying I'm sick of castles, kingdoms, and other exercises of antiquated dryness).  Its not revolutionary, airtight, or perfect.  But it is a whole lot of fun.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 1


Overall - 1

RATING: 9
The logic puzzle of managing what you have collected, organizing your traps, and sequencing which order to defeat your adventurers makes the title shine.  The choices of holding back a monster to prevent healing, leading the adventurers into a room or a hallway, picking which adventurer to attack, and ordering the traps to maximize damage.  This could become automatic in a few plays, but I think if that happens the central board play will develop more.  

The first time we played it took 4 hours including set up, clean up, and rules.  The second and third games clocked in at 100 minutes including set up/take down.  

This game always sits on my brain after its played.  The evilometer is a fun fun thing.  It requires players to remain relatively close to at least one other player to help aim for the adventurers they want, unless of course they feel lucky then they will go off on their own.  The paladin is a fun mechanic, and easily avoided in most games unless you choose to bring him over because the paladin is worth almost 2 adventurers.  The central board play is neat, because you need to be scanning your opponents boards.  Its frequently a game of chicken, because the 2nd and 3rd spots are frequently more valuable, but if the play is so obvious all 4 players will place there and you will be shut out with nothing to show for it.  The board is larger and getting shut out is less prevalent than I thought it would be after reading the rules, and one must put effort into getting 2nd and 3rd spots.  Keeping flexibility of resources while accruing a dangerous dungeon seems like the main difficulties I am currently facing. 

The game is a chore to teach, but fortunately simultaneous action selection works best with regular opponents in my opinion, so the difficulty teaching is negated somewhat.  I like it a lot right now, as it has parts of playing the system and playing off of the players.  Its my kind of game.

Truly envious of the design.  It is bulky at first, but after the second and third plays the rules are quite intuitive and consistent.  I'm not sure which rule I would change, because despite its "bulk", every exception and rule is not added for flavor but it increases the decisions and the worth of your choices.

I believe maintaining flexibility far outweights short term efficiency in the central board play.  Once you "need" any currency, you will be screwed.

I revisted this after having it on the shelf for awhile.  It is fiddly, but it holds up.

RATING: 6
TRADED AWAY (4/17/10) --- I want to like this game so much. I keep playing it again and again in the hope that I'll see the light. I'm up to 7 plays now (at every possible player count) and I'm ready to throw in the towel and give up. I really enjoy and highly respect a lot of Vlaada's games (particularly Galaxy Trucker, Through the Ages, and Prophecy), and I was really looking forward to Dungeon Lords, but its time to swallow the bitter pill of disappointment and move on. Dungeon Lords is a game that is too cumbersome for its own good. It is caught in the no man's land between Galaxy Trucker and Through the Ages, both excellent games in very different ways. I get bogged down in Dungeon Lords and can't enjoy the game. I enjoy the fast and loose gameplay of Galaxy Trucker, as well as the slow and thoughtful gameplay of Through the Ages, but Dungeon Lords feels as if it wants to have the fun of Galaxy Trucker along with the complexity of Through the Ages, and it's a mismatch, at least for my brain. Vlaada says that it's a game that works well for his group of friends, and I very much believe him. The chaos of the simultaneous action selection in Dungeon Lords might work well for a group that played it together a lot, but for someone looking for an occasional game with different groups of people, it's very unpredictable and frustrating (with severe penalties for incorrectly predicting your opponents' plans). I had initially hoped that the cumbersome nature of the game and the maintenance throughout the various phases would work more smoothly after playing the game many times, but after 7 plays I find myself still waiting. It's not the complexity that puts me off, since I love games like Die Macher and Antiquity, but rather the complexity combined with the randomness, theme, components, and feeling of the game. It just feels like a mismatch that I can't quite get past, and can't seem to find the game very fun. The game is constantly tripping over itself and pulling you in different directions with its seemingly conflicting goals and methods of achieving those goals. All that being said, I know when I'm having fun playing a board game, and this one just isn't fun.

Purchased: Essen 2009

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Got this because I liked my other two Vlaada games (TtA and Graenaland) and because of a possible similarity to In the Year of the Dragon with periodic impending disasters: In this case, adventurers trying to raid the dungeon you're building.

So, the rulebook starts you out with four combat training scenarios that are literally puzzles to solve, as they have no randomness. I found that fun, even though I normally don't like puzzles. I thought it would end up being relatively tolerable because there would be no guarantee that naturally-occurring combat scenarios would even [i]have[/i] a solution per se.

The game is divided into two "years." Each year consists of four rounds of trying to draft three actions at a time, followed by a combat phase where adventurers show up to each player's dungeon, and spend four rounds trying to fight their way in. I feel like there isn't enough of either half for the game to feel substantial.

When drafting your three actions, you're basically blindly programming where to send three workers, and everyone's plans are revealed at once. Things are set up so that you generally want to draft something later than others. What you get (or can exchange for) varies based on the order that you ended up drafting it relative to the other players. Commonly if you show up later, you can do more of a thing, but have to pay more to do it. For room drafting, there are only two to draft, and the first player to show up will get nothing if two subsequent players take both rooms.

I feel that you do this drafting too few times, and it's too chaotic. It seems like you just know vaguely what you want to get more of, and hope that you can get it. For my money this programmed system should be scrapped and replaced with something more traditional, that makes it an advantage to be the start player and draft what you want as soon as you can. I don't like this dynamic where you hold back from doing what you need because you hope someone will set you up for something better.

Then, the adventurers attacking your dungeon is less interesting than the training scenarios. I think they (the actually occurring scenarios) are generally [i]easier[/i], maybe as compensation for the designer knowing the preceding drafting phases are too chaotic. Not sure, but it seems extremely hit-or-miss whether you're going to face any interesting decisions or feel tension.

I can't help but like the deterministic combat rules, though. Fighters move to the front. Thieves reduce trap damage. Priests heal after a combat, and Wizards cast a spell that is specified by a card draw.

...Tried replacing the drafting rule, so that you place workers in turn, and can place under any open spot under an action (except for the monster/room spots which must be filled from the bottom). This reduces the chaos and I like it quite a bit better; the game is at least a 7 this way.

RATING: 6
I think that the biggest flaw with this game, is that its way too complex for its theme and most casual gaming groups. The game also makes use of dummy players for 2 and 3 player games, something that I dislike very much. It's probably not be that big of a deal drawing 3 cards and blocking 3 worker placement spaces in a 3 player game. But in a 2 player game, the dummy mechanics really slow the game down and makes it way too AP prone. 
In fact I would not recommend this game with 2 player at all.

Also if you think your going to be building up an epic dungeon, your going to be very disappointed. Dungeon Lords, like Kingsburg, is all about building up and trying to survive the end of the year, when the game attacks you. So when the game is over you will be sitting there with half a ruin of a half finished looking dungeon, hoping the other players did worse than you.

Finally must say that I find the rule book a mess.

RATING: 4
needs exactly 4 players, otherwise someone's playing the extra boards.  very good theme integration and components, decisions FEEL interesting but it's hard to really mess up- thus scores just kind of push to a similar range... the learning curve to roughly optimal play is very small, making it good for 1 or 2 plays only. the differences between good players will generally come down to guessing wrong during the blind action selection-  which is the primary interaction, and is very small due to the guesswork involved.  

I've literally watched a game played where 2 of the players selected their actions (about 10 seconds), and then either had to get up to help customers or had to have the other players resolve the actions because they didn't really know what was going on.  This to me was the epitome of the problem- there's about 10 seconds of decision making, then several minutes of almost absolute automation during resolution.  I'm ok with some downtime,  but not 2000%+ downtime compared to the interesting decision time.

summary:
superb thematic integration, low meaningful interaction, only 4 players, and your decisions will probably all come out in the wash.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Мне особенно понравилась механика баталий. На отлично. Сперва мы долго собираем армию и ловушки лавирую по злости, чтобы к тебе пришли правильные герои. А затем когда начинается бой, то можно голову сломать пытаясь понять: как тем что у тебя есть их завалить минимальными усилиями. Никаких кубиков нету - чистый расчет на основании способностей героев, ловушек и монстров (или привидений). Все монстры (и привидения) различаются далеко не просто силой, как и ловушки, не говоря про героев или заклинания. Например слизень может либо плюнуть слабо по всем, либо просто "наслизить" так, чтоб герои долго отмывались и потеряли время. Поэтому расчет боя интересный и нетривиальный. Процессу конечно мешают заклинания, которые колдуют волшебники. Если предварительно не побывать на уроках в магической академии, то могут ожидать сюрпризы.

Кстати - карты заклинаний единственная фишка, которая требует знания языка от игроков. Если они не могут прочитать на карте (ну или отличить/вспомнить) заклинание, то им бесполезно ходить в академию чтобы подсматривать эти карты. Впрочем это не критичный вопрос и их можно не подсматривать, просто они всегда будут с сюрпризом. :) А если страшно, то можно сделать ставку на скорейшую нейтрализации волшебников своими ловушками и монстрами (или привидениями). :)

Игра часто заставляет хохотать. Это и изначальный юмор из рулбука, и юморные заклинания вроде огненного шара (не буду спойлерить), и различные неудачи, которые в такой атмосфере переходят в шутки. Тема игры вообще очень сильная.

Классные подсказки по процессу хода. Передвигаем фишку фазы и делаем что на иконках нарисовано. Процесс вообще довольно быстрый, и фактически в игре две главные фазы: одновременная тайная раздача приказов во время строительства и одновременная раздача приказов во время битвы. Остальные действия по большей части выполняются автоматически. А всего за игру 4 фазы строительства в первый год игры, затем 4 фазы боя, и аналогично на второй год игры. Итого всего 8 ходов на развитие подземелья, а за ход дается 3 приказа (по типу как в Агриколе)

Агрикола однако играется намного проще (по крайней мере семейный вариант). Там ставишь рабочего и тут же получаешь что надо. Тут же надо сразу 3 приказа спланировать тайно. После чего приказы всех игроков вскрываются и результат может несколько отличаться от желаемого. Хуже того, надо не просто угадывать кто куда пойдет, но и кто каким придет по счету. Может быть важно придти именно вторым, а не третьим, первым или конечно четвертым. Мало того, два из трех приказов прошлого хода нельзя повторять в следующем. Ум за разум заходит легко и непринужденно. (Ну если конечно думать, что необязательно) :)

Игра идет на очки, но этого не замечаешь. Как и в Агриколе их дают почти за все, но тема настолько яркая, а баталии жесткие, что на очки можно и забить не жалея. Их можно даже и не считать, если играть типа как семейный вариант.

RATING: 6
Vlaada has found a new way for players to manage a ridiculous amount of chaos in his new game, this time simplifying the process down to a worker placement/resource management type of game.  The game is very complicated, but easy to play; the rules fiddliness is what accounts for the inherent chaos.  The game should be played with 4 players only, though, since any players fewer than 4 are replaced by dummy players whose actions each round are randomly chosen and whose minions are placed on the level 2 action of whichever cards are chosen.  It works fairly well, without having to keep up with various actions for the dummy player, but its unpredictability and randomness can be a little frustrating.

1 play: 3 players
3 plays: 4 players

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Vlaada Chvátil works his magic on another classic PC series. While not quite as good as Through the Ages, this take on the cult classic Dungeon Keeper is still a very good game. This re-imagined version is basically a worker placement game. The workers are of course imps...and adorable imps, at that. Using these imps, players construct and populate a dungeon with monsters. The goal being to build the most evil lair in the land. Unlike other worker placement games however, this game fights back. At the end of the round, a party of adventurers invades each player's creation. This portion of the game is something that most people will either love or hate. The dungeon raid plays out like a puzzle, with each player trying to utilize his dungeon rooms, traps, and monsters to destroy the encroaching party. I personally enjoy this phase of the game, as it is usually quite the brain burner. Some people find the switch to solitaire mode a bit jarring, but my group  enjoys watching everyone's dungeons fail spectacularly...it's usually good for a lot of laughs.

Speaking of laughs, Dungeon Lords has one the most entertaining rulebooks I have ever had the pleasure of reading. It does a good job of not only teaching you the game in an enjoyable manner, but also in justifying why certain spaces have fluctuating rewards. In general, it's best to be the SECOND player to choose a given action. The rulebook explains why this is so, usually in comedic fashion. The overall effect is that it makes a lot of the game balance mechanisms feel a bit less artificial.

The main reason I don't rate this as highly as Through the Ages is that I don't believe it has as much replay value. You are playing against the game more than against the other players. After a few runs through the hero fights there aren't a whole lot of surprises left for you. There is actually some rudimentary control over which adventurer attacks which dungeon and you can create some interaction by manipulating this...but it requires so much analysis to pull off that it clashes with the fun theme the game works so hard to create. This brings up another issue as well. For a game with such a whimsical theme, it is incredibly difficult to teach. Learning how to fight the invading heroes takes a good 45 minutes, as players need to understand how each character fights and by extension, how to counter them. This isn't a big deal in a regular gaming group, but trust me, teaching this part of the game over and over gets incredibly tiresome.

I love Dungeon Lords, but it is seriously hard to get it to the table, because of the steep learning curve. Frankly, I've become to lazy to teach it anymore...It's too bad, because it really is a fun experience.

RATING: 6
This is a very well-designed game, but it hits two of my biggest pet peeves in gaming. For one thing, depending on how things play out, the entire second year of combat could be utterly pointless, since one player may be enough in the lead that it doesn't matter - and, yet, combat can take a while with AP players (most of the people I play with, and I have a touch of it now and then). The second, more major one is that I hate being locked into decisions based on unknown information, like you are with your second and third order each turn. These make it not fun for me, but I can definitely understand why so many enjoy this.

RATING: 6
The premise of the game is building a dungeon and populating it with monsters and traps, but the core mechanic is an unforgiving blind auction/action selection.

Imagine Agricola, but instead of selecting actions one at a time, everyone secretly selects in advance and ties are broken by rock-paper-scissors. Good luck trying to use a bread-baking strategy.

That's what Dungeon Lords is: Pure chaos. Players expecting to find a game about building the best dungeon are going to be disappointed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Fun on a bun!

Based on early reviews I had written this one off for my game group as too complex.  I like complex games just fine, but usually can't get them to the table if they're for more than two players (and even then it's tough).  Finally got to play Heidi and Jason's copy and it's straight back to the wish list!  This game is not nearly as complex as it has been made out to be.  If you can play Galaxy Trucker, you can play this.  If you can play Agricola, you're way ahead of the game.

There are two main phases to the game: building and populating your dungeon, and fighting off the adventurers.  The first phase is heavily reliant on simultaneous action selection.  I love this mechanic, but there are a lot that hate it, so if that's you know that this is probably half the game.

If you've played Galaxy Trucker, fighting off the adventurers feels similar to the surviving-the-flight phase of that game, but with a lot more strategy.  The decisions in the survival phase of Galaxy Trucker are mostly trivial, but in Dungeon Lords it can be surprisingly deep and analytical.  The introductory puzzles show this off beautifully.  If you're worried about AP, it can certainly strike here.  I was very pleasantly surprised by this aspect of the game.

Anyway, I'm excited about Dungeon Lords.  If I had disposable income and closet space at the moment, I would have already placed my order.  Unfortunately I'll have to wait until the next gift-giving holiday for an excuse.

RATING: 8.3
Warning, this is not a gateway Euro to start out new gamers on.  This is a game reminiscent of the computer game Dungeon Keeper where you get to try out your ability to manage a dungeon and fight off the annoying adventurers who keep coming in and messing up you crib.  There's really a good planning element to the game in deciding what order to play traps and monsters (or ghosts) to whomp on the intruders and keep from losing dungeon tiles to them.  All the while you need to manage your food and gold resources to keep up with taxes to the Ministry of Dungeons.  There's enough randomness to keep it from being a gamer's game but it certainly has a lot going on to keep you thinking about what you want to acquire and how to manage what you have.

RATING: 7
[b]Pillars of the Earth[/b]: Don't place workers in sequence, pull them from a bag instead.
[b]Stone Age[/b]: Don't collect specific benefits, to determine the amount roll dice instead.
[b]Vasco de Gama[/b]: Don't pay an action cost, randomly pay that amount [+3,-3] instead.
[b]Carson City[/b]: Don't expect a selected action, duel Games of Thrones style for it instead.
[b]Dungeon Lords[/b]: Don't anticipate some payout from a square, randomly join a queue determined by how many people selected the action and in what order instead.

Remember Set? The game of similarity recognition slash verification of your dog's epilepsy? (Or lack thereof.) Well, I see a set. I don't know why this set's shared characteristic is desirable, but I see the set. In all of these post-Caylus worker placement games, this action you think you're getting... you're not actually getting it.

What's funny about Dungeon Lords is that the gimmicky Robo Rally-esque blind action programming only impedes the flavorful, reasonably thought-provoking worker placement system. It's complex enough to create and defend your own dungeon; traditional worker placement would suffice. Normally it's simplistic games that need this kind of indirect, lottery-style "system" - think Trajan, where acquiring VP's is very straightforward and obvious, hence the system of trays to brute force inefficiency into the process.

Nevertheless Dungeon Lords is pretty enjoyable. Traps v. Monsters is an interesting dilemma, as Monsters stick around for both fights and score end-game points, but have upkeep costs and limit one per tunnel, while Traps are one-time use and wonderful for tunnels, if not rooms. The Evilometer is a great touch, but the annuity benefits from allocating imps to rooms is vital, yet the process for acquiring these rooms is hopelessly chaotic. With "locked" actions, it's unlikely an area will exceed its capacity of three (i.e. all players choose it), but the most important part of the game only has a capacity of two. Moreover, the first guy present gets ousted by the next two players, and the fourth guy gets shafted because of filled capacity, meaning everyone walks the same tightrope of too-early, too-late musical chairs. It's a bluff-double-bluff gamble because waiting until next turn might also fail if players put room in their first slot to avoid locking it.

The dilemma of Age of Empires III and Caylus: "If only I could get what I want, when I want it." (Turns are fragmented into worker placements, actions must be selected carefully to preserve long-term strategy with regard to the best quality actions present and leaving those to an opponent.)

The dilemma of Dungeon Lords et al: "If only I could get what I want, after I'm already entitled to it."

RATING: 8
as fun and confusing as i thought it would be.  evokes dungeon keeper, while being also it's own game.  also evokes several other modern euros... Agricola, i'm looking at you.  has a high difficulty curve, due to the interaction between resource gathering and the battles.  great graphics and tone, and bits.  The most fun and challenging bit is the action selection mechanism.  It is wonderful and complex, and thought-provoking.  This is where the true miracle of the game happens.  If you just plop down your three actions without deep thought about how you want them to play out, and how they will interact with the other player's choices, you will lose.

RATING: 3
I might have to call this a "blind worker placement game", sure you can sort of figure out what your opponents are up to, and block or avoid those actions, but sadly in gameplay this is so sloooow... There's the waiting for everyone to be happy with their 3 cards, displaying them 1 at a darn time and placing our Minions on the actions, then resolving them 1 at a time in the same order each time. All that and no direct interaction at all, unless you play with the Magic Items - sigh!

For me it's too slow, with not enought direct confrontation or enough interaction. If someone plays poorly and lags behind, there's nothing they can do, but hope that the person in the lead screws up as well.

The game has a fun and funny theme, and breakinig up the gameplay with puzzle-solving seems cool, but again just leads to lots of waiting on everyone to complete their puzzles.

It is also a hog to teach someone, with lots of rules (even if they are well written and funny) and unless you play with players of near equal skill, it becomes highly chaotic, just like with blind bidding games.

I like this less with each play.

[b]The Treasure Chest full of platinum coins and diamond swans:[/b]

1. Beautifully manufactured game.

3. Consistent art.
4. Great theme.

[b]The festering pool of bulbous-eyed slime:[/b]

1. Too fiddly for 2 players.
2. A massive theme misfire, a WASTE of theme.
3. You build the smallest dungeon known to man or troll.
4. Overwrought rules.

7. Boring puzzle-solving combat.
8. Not enough hack and slash.

10. Way too long to play for the pay-off.
11. You seem to be managing decline, rather than assembling a Dungeon of the Bear or Uncle Ugly's Underground type of super-dungeon.
12. I'm programmed to love games like this, so it hurts doubly when it's as flat as this baby.
13. I found the game frustrating on many, many levels, continually wishing I was playing another game. Another game exactly like this one, but with "fun" added, and a good half of the fat butchered away.
14. Cutesy-poo art. Extremely well done cutesy-poo, but too whimsical and cute for this old, Tunnels and Trolls DM.

RATING: 6
Vlaada Chvátil is truly a talented man.  The way he is able to inject unique concepts into different genre of games is really a sight to behold, and I do appreciate his take on them.  However, other than Through the Ages, everything else since then just have not hit the right chord and thus felt flat to me.

In his latest design, Dungeon Lords pit the players against generic heroes, which I suppose is serviceable but ultimately boring (and static, making replayability a problem), once you figure out the correct way of dealing with each class.  The simultaneous action selection is only interesting because two of your choices become unavailable every other round which serve as limited open information to everyone else.  There is a bit of strategy involved in guessing what actions other people will select and in what order, but there is still a big dose of randomness to it.

The building of the dungeon itself is disappointing.  There is a lack of variety in room tiles that will actually help in the defense of your lair; the questionable usefulness of trap cards; and the inability to make legal loops or place rooms next to each other make no sense to me.

In the end, you may even consider Dungeon Lords a kind of a rip off of Kingsburg.  You go through the same exact four seasons in preparation for an impending disaster (a party of adventurers rather than barbarian hordes).  Instead of dice rolling to gather resources you flip cards, both has a worker placement style with one offering a more interesting mechanic than the other.  You know, now that I think about it, the structure is awfully similar.  I guess it's no wonder I didn't care for it, since I didn't really like Kingsburg either.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Competition over opportunities make up the bulk of player interaction, interspersed with puzzle-like combat sessions against parties of random adventurers following a few simple rules of conduct (fighters always go to the front of the party, etc.). It makes for a competitive game, but one which still generates a certain amount of camaraderie among the aspiring Dungeon Lords, who all must suffer the bane of wandering adventurers, and the much more terrifying tax collector.

RATING: 9.2
弗拉达先生Vlaada Chvátil设计的作品永远都是那么的出人意料，地城领主将十多年前的电脑游戏地下城守护者完美的搬到了桌面游戏平台上。游戏规则幽默诙谐，过程体验忠实原作。玩家在游戏中主要扮演地下城主，招兵买马抵御嚣张的英雄前来破坏掠劫，但地城经营也非常重要，毕竟没有人会给你白白干活。。。如果版图游戏主要分为德式桌游和美式桌游两大类的话，我要说弗拉达先生的作品特点鲜明，独立自成一派。
【关键词】小魔怪/侍从，地下城陷阱/怪物，战士/盗贼/法师/牧师，圣骑士，隧道/房间，邪恶度，头衔，可见行动卡，有限行动位，美工插图，战斗结算方式

【推荐度】 高 【工人放置管理，中重度以上爱好者
